LESSON #1: BASIC COMPING RHYTHM

Comping is the main form of chordal accompaniment in straight-ahead jazz and is usually quite variable in its rhythms and
chord voicings. Like single-note improvisation, it involves constant choices on the part of the musician and requires a feel for
the style.

This kind of chordal texture is distinct from the all-quarter-notes “straight-four"/“four-to-the-bar” approach that also has its
place in the toolbox of the jazz guitarist. It sounds different as well from the big back-and-forth strumming that is so natural to
guitar in other styles. Here we look at a couple of key elements in a normal “comp” and try some exercises that can help us get
off the ground with this kind of chording rhythm.

One important observation is that there's a strong emphasis on upbeats in this kind of playing (the “ands” of each beat we
count in a measure). And along with that, many chords that we think of as coming in on beats 1 or 3 of a 4/4 measure can
actually get anticipated, or played one short 8th note earlier. Let's try this with a standard set of changes. Note that, for this
first example, the basic chords of the progression are named right on top of the staff, as we might find them on a lead sheet to
2 tune, while a suggested embellished version of each is shown above that in a fretboard diagram:

EXAMPLE 1

i “Am? Db? Abm7 Db7 Cmaj7
= == — = — ——— = ee =:
= 7 = =

When first learning a progression like this one, we may instinctively practice the chords using all whole notes or half notes,
entering right on the downbeat, as a reasonable way to get them under our fingers. This in itself would not give the song
much of a swinging feel, but by hitting each chord on the “and” before its official time, we already take a step towards viable
jazz comping. (Remember that if it’s part of an overall swung groove, the “and is later in the beat!)

Next, we try a different basic exercise towards good chordal rhythm in jazz, which is to choose a simple rhythmic figure that
fits the feel of the music and repeat it through a progression. Here we have a one-bar figure, which, with only two notes,
demonstrates another key characteristic of comping: there is usually a mix of downbeats and upbeats, and also of sustained
and short chords. Notice that, when the harmonic pace changes (two chords per measure instead of one), we continue with
the same rhythm, fitting each new chord into it as we go. This means an anticipation again for D9 and D9 in measures 5 and
6. In order to focus mainly on the rhythm, we'll continue using the same chord shapes we had in Example 1 (now shown in the
tab below).

In the long run, listening to great players is what gives us a sense of how this is done (whether they play guitar, piano, or
another chordal instrument), and listening to those we play with is important in tailoring our comp to the situation. But with
some nice chord voicings, and a taste of basic jazz rhythms, we can get our comping underway.

TURNAROUND IMPROV

A typical turnaround in a chord progression often consists of I-VI-ii-V-I harmonic movement, which, for example, could mean
B»6-G7-Cm7-F7-B+6 in the key of B> (G7 being a common substitute for the Gm7 that occurs naturally in the B> major scale).
We call it a turnaround because it so frequently functions to bring us to the top of the tune again, from the end of one chorus
of a repeating song form. But this chord sequence is quite frequently found at the beginning or in the middle of a song—
sometimes twice or more in a row—and we would do well as improvisers to consider how to approach it. This is especially
true when the changes last only two beats each at a bright tempo!

Here's a basic outline of this sequence for a major-key version in jazz, played twice in a row with two beats per chord, with
possible chord shapes. We'll stay very simple these first two measures, then vary it a bit for the next go-around with a different
kind of major chord for | and+9ths on our VI7 and V7 chords.

EXAMPLE 1

TRACK 2

Notice that, even though we never leave the B> major scale, you can hear a sense of moving away from the home chord and
back again in the line as the changes progress. That's because this idea was constructed in such a way as to reflect the sound
of the basic Bb, Cm7, and F7 chords along the way, even if it kind of coasts through G7.

Once familiar with some of the ways we can get through these chords in our linear improvisation, it may be more natural

to mix our methods a bit, playing now more simply, and now more specifically, with a bit of space along the way between
phrases. In any case, learning to work with this I|-VI-ii-V-I motion can be helpful for many situations.

LESSON #3: THE MAJOR II-V-I

The harmonic movement known as a ii-V-I is one of the main building blocks of chord progressions found in jazz, as well as
many other styles. It is tremendously helpful for an improviser to recognize this grouping of chords when it comes along in a
tune and to have an idea of how to play on it as a unit.

It is called “two-five-one" after the tones of the scale (for a given key) that are the roots of each chord. The naturally occurring
ii-V-I in a major key, using seventh chords, involves a minor seven for ii, a dominant seven for V, and a major seven for |. Here’s
a very normal ii-V-I in C major, along with a C major scale for reference.

Dm7 G7 Cmaj7 . I F. a
4 10fe TT #) Sir 14) Sir a — —-
iy er” Bt SSS
2 333 5241 1 342
ae 5 a ae a a | i
2 - a _ B 7 3 0 7 9 10
6 10 —

If we were to approach improvisation on this chord movement by way of the most obvious scales, we might choose D Dorian
for Dm7, G Mixolydian for G7, and C major for Cmaj7—only to realize that these are really all the same set of tones (modally
related to each other)! We could indeed solo on these changes using only notes of the C major scale, but we'd do best to have
some idea of what is actually in each chord, and how they move from one to another. Otherwise, while thinking only of one
bunch of notes to play from, we may never achieve a sense of ii-V-1 motion within our linear ideas.

Let's get a look at the basic content of these three chords in arpeggio form (the root, 3rd, Sth, and 7th of each, in order):

Cmaj? Chord Tones Dim? Chord Tones G7 Chord Tones

—————
SS

We can experiment, for starters, with lines that use only these chord tones for each as it goes by, like this one (realize that we
just looked at them in only one octave, and here these notes may be ina higher range):

EXAMPLE 1

1 J2120 (33-13)

Dm7 G7 Cmaj?

Notice that there's a tendency for the 7th of a chord to lead to the 3rd of the next one, whether directly, or bouncing off of
another note in between.

Next we'll expand our options by including other notes from the scale while still emphasizing the sound of the individual
chords. Most often, these additional scalar tones can be seen as color tones of the basic chords, anyway (A is the 9th for a G7
or a 6th for a C major chord, while E can be a 9th on Dm/7, etc.).

Here we see another important factor to take into account as we work out ii-V-I ideas: these changes may occur in a quick
two-bar framework (two beats apiece for the ii and the V), or they may be stretched out over a longer span with one measure
apiece for those first two chords. There are other kinds of pacing yet, but these two options cover a lot of ground.

As one way to break out of the pure scale of the key, the V7 chord may well be altered instead of natural, with flatted or
sharped tones (and therefore no longer fitting into that major scale). Here’s an example of how the ii-V-I may sound in the
key of C with an altered G7 and a scale we could turn to for improv on the new chord:

the chords Gm7-C7-Fmaj7, we have just come across a ii-V-I in F within the tune,

chords or scales. The ii-V-I's in this progression require different kinds of pacing, as well as moving around to different key
areas.

10

(Ble) Ee THE MINOR II-V-I

The ii-V-i unit of chord movement in minor, just like the major-key version of the same thing, is an extremely common
element in the harmonic progressions of jazz and other styles. Also like the major ii-V-I, it uses chords built on the Ist, 2nd,
and Sth degrees of the scale for the key (hence the name “two-five-one").

Here's a typical ii-V-i in the key of A minor. Notice that the ii chord is a minor seven with ab5th (sometimes referred to as “half-
diminished"), the V chord is an altered dominant (at least with a flatted or raised 9th instead of a natural 9th), and the i chord
is a minor seven:

Bm7b5 E7b9 Am7
FE ot toe Sa

Str

2 341 2131 2 333

5 war Lf

And here are a few versions of the minor scale, which may be pertinent to our progression:

A Harmonic Minor Scale A Natural Minor Scale A Melodic Minor Scale (Ascending)

= + fe? _ —* ei > SS
é ————— [be =— {E=——* = —— sy

”

Notice that, with any of these, we get the same first five notes (including the notes that will at least be the root of our “two,
“five,” and “one” chords). But it can be a little trickier in minor to point to a scale that will totally encompass the tones of these
chords. The harmonic minor scale can account for them all if we go with a m(maj7) chord for our tonic (i chord), which is an
option. Natural minor will give us a minor seven chord for i, but not a dominant seven on V. Melodic minor can work if a m6 or
m(maj7) chord is used for i, but we won't find the complete m7+5 chord within it, rooted on the 2nd degree.

All those scales could play a part in improvisation over the ii-V-i chord grouping, but some others that are typically thought of
for this purpose are the Locrian scale for the iim7+5 (amounts to the same notes as natural minor from the tonic), the altered
(or super Locrian) for the V7, and the Dorian for the im7.

B Locrian Mode E Altered (Super Locrian) Scale A Dorian Mode

LESSON #5: COMPIN’ THE BLUES

The blues form is one of the staples of the jazz tradition, and guitarists of this style need to know it, play it in different keys,
and be aware of some particular variations on the changes. When jazz musicians play a blues, a 12-bar form is still the norm,
but the usual progression is not quite the same three-chord I-IV-V cycle commonly found in rock or the blues genre itself—
related, but at least a little more involved.

In this lesson, we'll look at two examples of chording through a Bb blues with a basic jazz progression. For both of these, | will
comp on the recording with some normal variation in rhythm (where you see slash marks in the staff just to mark the time).

Example 1 outlines the changes as they are typically thought of in jazz, with ii-V chord motion (Cm7 to F7) where we might
have expected V-IV (F7 to B:7), and a VI chord thrown in to boot (G7). For the moment, we'll stick to very plain seventh chords.
Though the overall sound may be a little bland for actual performance, these will help us to get a handle on the main points
and give us a good basis for the more colorful voicings we can plug in later on.

EXAMPLE 1
TRACK 5

Example 2 fleshes out this progression with different versions of each chord (or substitutions) and additional chords along

commonly heard embellishments, and we'll use mostly richer-sounding voicings to put it all together nicely.

EXAMPLE 2

This is still just an example of how it may go, as further potential variations abound in the overall progression, and in chord
voicings. You would unlikely see all these particulars on paper for a 12-bar blues tune in the style—if you're reading it at all. A
jazz musician generally understands the basics of the form and some possibilities of what might happen along the way. This
underscores an important principle that applies to many tunes in an improvisatory jazz setting, though it is especially true
of a blues: the official chord progression may be just a framework—a harmonic direction or set of possibilities—more than a
super-specific list of chords to be played on each go-around.

Also included in the jazz repertoire are some more fundamentally different versions of the blues form, sometimes with very
unusual chords, or in minor, or beyond a 12-bar structure, or even just using the same good old I-IV-V progression found in
other styles after all! But with the examples in this lesson, you have a notion of what's probably in store when a fellow jazzer
says, “Let's play a blues.”

14

Ble) EE i-ag CHORD-TONIN’ THE BLUES

Many improvisers, if unfamiliar with jazz language and chord progressions, will approach a blues as a thing to be soloed on
primarily with minor pentatonic or blues scale material. And that, in and of itself, is fine to some extent—especially if those
tones are put together with a good sense of phrasing and rhythmic feel for the style. But the jazz player also needs to know
how to address the changing harmonies of a blues progression, as much as (or perhaps more than) any other set of changes.

To start, we need an understanding of how this 12-bar form generally appears within the style. Unlike its usual incarnation in
the blues genre per se, or in rock, a jazz version of the blues typically has more than just the |, |V, and V chords of the key and
looks something like this in the key of Bb.

Bb7 Bb7 Bb7 £b7

poe

Bb7 G7 Cm7 F7 Bb? (G7 Cm7_——sF7) Bb7
PSS L LDP AP PP PAP DSP A ISS PD PAD AP DAP PAP PPP

e

There are still plenty of the | and IV (B7 and £7) chords here, until about halfway through, and then VI, ii, and V get involved
(G7, Cm7, F7), often coming back as part of a turnaround at the end of the form, to bring us back to the top.

An important step to being able to improvise on the chords is to be clear on what notes are actually in the chords. In this
lesson, we try, as an exercise, to solo on the progression with basic chord tones only and nothing else. In the process, we
can begin to map out these chordal members on the fretboard (beyond where they sit in familiar chord shapes) and start to
hear the chord changes in our single-note lines. It may seem like quite a limiting way to play—and it is—but it’s a worthwhile
challenge for guitarists of different levels. If you haven't played changes much, it keeps you on your toes to switch gears as
needed, and if you already have a repertoire of hipper lines, it forces you to see what you can do with this very narrow set of
tones instead!

To be prepared for this exercise, let's first look at the actual makeup of the chords and one way each to render them as
arpeggios on the guitar, fairly close to each other on the neck. In all these cases, we really just use the first, third, fifth, and
seventh tones of a scale—Mixolydian for the dominant seventh chords and Dorian for the minor seventh—which become the
root, 3rd, 5th, and 7th of the chord:

licks), since in the long run we will surely allow ourselves to use other tones of a scale, more complex versions of the chords,

and beyond, as well as musical ideas from entirely different sources. But it can open the door to true involvement with chord
changes in our melodic playing.

TRITONE SUBSTITUTION

Tritone substitution is the replacement of a chord with another one of the same kind rooted a tritone away from the original
(a distance of three whole steps). It most clearly applies to dominant seven chords and has the effect of turning a natural
dominant seventh into a fully altered dominant.

A simple 715 chord is the place where a dominant chord and its tritone substitute meet—where they are note-for-note the
same, although in theory each tone is now a different member of the chord with some enharmonic spellings. We'll start here
with a very plain B+7 and E7 and show how, by flatting their Sths, we make them identical:

Bb7 Bb7bs E7 E7bs/Bb
pigeon faa ue presi el ste aes
1 283 2 341 3241 vt 2 341
+Sth a Root
re ges =
os Se — == ——S
© bo — _ borer. Root > ee at Ao

This is to show what these chords have in common, but of course the principle of tritone substitution is useful only if it yields
a different sound. And this is usually done by swapping out one unaltered dominant chord (like plain old B+7, or B+9 or B+13)
for another (such as E9 or E13, perhaps with a 411th). The result is often that, where we had a V-I resolution in a progression
(8-7 heading to Bmaj7, for example), we now have a chromatic root movement to get where we're going—actually a pretty
common sound in many styles and tunes. Here's an example with a ii-V-l in the key of B:

Despite being so closely identified with the altered version of the original chords, tritone subs give us a different way of thinking
about certain progressions, both in chordal playing and melodic improvisation, and open the door to new possibilities.

Suppose we now try two improv ideas for this short progression, each time using only basic scale or chord tones—first
thinking Fm7-B+7-E,, then Fm7-E7-B: (with tritone substitution). For the first version, we'll start with a prototypical ii-V-I kind
of line—one which heads up through the Fm7 arpeggio and back down through the Bb Mixolydian scale (same as the Dorian
scale on F) before heading into tones of Ebmaj7. In the tritone sub version, we'll descend through scalar tones 5-3-2-1 for
each chord, resulting in an overall down-by-half-steps sound to the whole thing.

There's a strong connection between a minor seven chord and the natural dominant rooted a perfect 4th above it, often

will always contain an A»).

line, whether or not that particular harmony has been laid out by their bandmates or in the song itself.

Tritone substitution is part of the language of jazz, and with some study you can learn to recognize when it is built into a
tune and use it creatively in your own playing.

Ble) Et: TRITONE TURNAROUNDS

In this lesson we look at some common variations, derived by the use of tritone substitution, of the turnaround chords
found at the end of a tune (or sometimes in the middle). This technique usually means replacing a dominant seventh chord
with another one that is three whole steps away, and we'll check out how it can add interest both to our soloing and to our
chording in these situations.

First let's consider a typical turnaround for a Bb blues—found in the last two measures of the form and bringing us around
from B-7 to B+7 again at the top of the next chorus—involving classic !|-VI-ii-V-| chord movement. For a new angle on it, we'll
use tritone subs on the VI and V chords and take a little liberty with the ii chord, making it a dominant seven as well, where it
might otherwise have been minor (while using the nifty thirteen chord version of each dominant seven):

The D+13 used in place of a G7#9 results in virtually the same chord (with a different bass note, perhaps), but still the overall
concept leads to a new sound for our turnaround, with chromatic, parallel movement of this dominant thirteen voicing. It may
seem like random fret-by-fret sliding around with one chord shape, but it is not—it is logically linked to the more circle-of-
fifths-based motion that was there in the first place, and it functions similarly in terms of harmonic direction—how it moves
away from the | chord and brings us home again.

An improviser can run with the implications of the substituted progression, thinking of the new chords as the basis for their
ideas, maybe even while the regular chords are being played. For instance, we could start with a short idea that fits a natural
B-7 very cleanly (descending with tones 2, 7, 6, and 5 in the B+ Mixolydian scale) on the top two strings and simply move it up
and back down again to fit the shifting dominant seven chords.

LESSON #9: MINOR BLUES

A common alternate type of 12-bar blues form, in jazz as well as in the blues genre itself, is the minor blues. Like the more
dominant-seven-based “regular” blues, it is subject to many variations and can appear with simpler or more complex versions
of the progression. As we compare it to the main |, IV, and V chord structure of the standard blues, the i and iv typically become
minor, while the V remains dominant and is often preceded by the VI chord, which is also a dominant seventh.

For a G minor blues, that could mean that Gm/7, Cm/7, B:7 (the LVI), and D7 are involved, as in this basic outline of the form.
We'll also let G minor turn into G7 in measure 4, accentuating the move to C minor, and we can comp with the usual variety of
chordal rhythm, using these shapes:

Notice these particular details, which are representative of how jazzers often treat a minor blues, and which can help you
apply more chord shapes and play it in different keys:

» Aminor six is frequently used for the tonic chord (the i chord) instead of a minor seven. That opens the door to the

minor/major seven chord as well, as both it and the minor six relate well to the melodic minor scale (as opposed to
Dorian for a minor seven).

Along the way, some “mini-turnarounds,” or brief ii-V-I movements, can provide extra momentum into the next chord
or break up longer spans of a single chord. That is the case here with the Am7+5 and D7 in measures 2 or 6, bringing
us back to Gm6, and the Dm7+5 and G7 in measure 4, heading to Cm7 (a ii-V-i in C minor).

Where we see B9 to D7#9, this reflects the normal pattern of a natural dominant for the sVI chord leading to an altered
dominant for the V. Another option here would be B13 leading to D745, for example.

A little more complicated and specific yet is the march down from G minor to Es7 in measures 7-8. We could look at
this as really being a circle-of-fifths kind of progression to connect those chords, including some tritone substitution.
That is, it’s as if it were iii-VI-ii-V-1 motion in the key of B, or Gm7-C7-Fm7-B:7-B,, except with G7 replacing C7 and
E7 replacing B+7. That's quite a mouthful there, but in any case, we wind up with a nice harmonic flow down in half
steps. Notice here, too, the pattern of natural versus altered dominant chords.

The minor blues is an important part of the jazz musician’s repertoire, full of harmonic possibilities for chording and soloing.

AIM FOR THE CHANGE

When improvising over chord changes, we may well think of the scales or chord tones that fit the situation as we go. But we
can take this one notch further and seek to dig into the progression by emphasizing the notes that most bring out the change
from chord to chord. With some awareness of which tones these are for a certain tune, we can aim for them at key places
within our melodic lines.

For an example, let’s look at what a few of these tones might be for a G minor blues. We'll work with this version of the
changes:

Ma CONSTANT PHRASE
sae SHAPE EXERCISE

Our focus as jazz improvisers may often be on choice of notes and the harmonic content of what we play, even while we fully
intend to get into the rhythmic feel of the tune. But an important perspective on rhythm, use of space, making a musical
statement, and even on note choice itself, can be gained by thinking of phrase shape first.

In this lesson, we'll try an exercise in which we choose a short melodic rhythm to play repeatedly through a song form or
chord progression. We come up with it without being specific about pitch and then fill in the blanks with notes as we go,
appropriate to the harmonies at hand. The key element is that the rhythmic figure must stay the same, and the notes must fit
into its framework.

Let's start with this two-bar rhythmic phrase as an example (remember, it's shown without actual pitches on purpose). Notice
that it starts on the “and” of beat 2 as we count through the first measure, with five 8th notes in a row, and that the second
measure is all sustain or rest. When playing through this or any such rhythm, be sure to leave space where it belongs!

arardlIyTu - |

Now we play it repeatedly, using actual notes and changing them each time to create new phrases with the same rhythmic
shape. To warm up, we'll use the harmonic structure of a D Dorian modal vamp (on Dm/7), so we won't have to worry about
switching the set of tones we choose from along the way. Here's a refresher on that scale, followed by what | came up with
using our chosen phrase shape through eight measures:

EXAMPLE 1

D Dorian

When trying this process on your own, you may want to spend some time reviewing whatever scalar or chordal material you'll
be working with, depending on how solid you feel with it, before you start in real time with the specified rhythmic shape.
Once we're rolling, we want to be consistent with the figure we've decided upon and not get held back in a struggle to locate
viable notes. We could also limit the range of tones we'll draw from—maybe to three strings and one fretboard position—if
this is helpful for starters.

Plus, as much as this exercise would seem to mean a shift of focus from notes to rhythm, it really has everything to do with
note choice after all. It forces us to listen for which tones of the present scale or chord sound good to us within that limited
shape, if we pursue it with that concern in mind.

100 JAZZ LESSONS | GUITAR LESSON GOLDMINE
For the next stage of our exercise, let's try a new two-bar rhythm. As you come up with phrase shapes yourself in this process,
you could arrive at them in a written-out sort of way, thinking of them in terms of quarter notes or 8ths, and how they are

counted within a measure, etc. But better yet is to imagine them just by their sound at first, if you can. The last note in this
rhythm is marked to be played a bit short.

eid Fl de |

This time we'll apply the shape over changing chords, where the notes we want to draw from may be different with each
repetition, even if just slightly. To get started, we'll use a progression that has a new chord just every two measures (mostly),
which will match up conveniently with our two-bar phrases, But a chord change could certainly take place in the middle of
our rhythmic shape—pay attention as this happens in the last two measures of Example 2, below. We can continue thinking

of D Dorian for the Dm/7, and otherwise we'll need A Dorian for Am7 and Bb Lydian dominant for the Bb7. Here's a review of
those last two scales;

Beyond being a drill limited to our practice time, this exercise represents a mindset that we can adopt in our improvisation,

musical idea is really a composite of tones and timing.

MB CHANGING RHYTHMIC
eel SHAPE EXERCISE

In improvisation, one hazard of the ongoing search for notes can be a sort of formlessness, in which our playing sounds more
like a run-on stream of tones than musical ideas. Here's an exercise to help us shape the note-material of our solos into viable
phrases and see the rhythmic variety with which this can be done.

We begin by selecting a series of tones that can work over a given harmony, in the order we will play them for a single musical
idea (not just a scale to be drawn from), We'll stick with a reasonable number of notes that could fit easily enough into one or
two measures. Next, we see how many different ways we can come up with to rhythmically shape this row of tones, so as to
make a nice statement within a particular groove and within the scope of one, two, or a few measures,

100 JAZZ LESSONS | GUITAR LESSON GOLDMINE 27

CALL AND RESPONSE

A common pattern of musical phrasing, especially in the African-American tradition, is call and response. The term stems from
the kind of situation in which one singer, or leader, routinely puts forth a short line of a song (perhaps a question) and is
answered by a chorus or congregation. But the influence of this phenomenon can be felt in other formats. Many a blues tune
is written such that the first two thirds of the 12-bar form both contain the same phrase (or similar), and the melodic line of the
last four-bar segment answers the first two, with words as well as tones.

This kind of pattern also takes place in instrumental and improvised music and can inspire a certain way of shaping things
even in our individual jazz solos. In general, it's often good that one phrase or idea seems to naturally follow the previous one,
giving a nice sense of melodic development, but you can sometimes take this a step further and deliberately try for a sort of
conversation with yourself in your own improvisation.

Structurally, this could happen in different ways. It doesn’t need to be in the context of a 12-bar blues, but some kind of
symmetrical form is helpful, like so many tunes have, with four- or eight-bar sections that can easily be divided into one-, two-,
or four-bar chunks for our call and response.

Here's an example of one way to do this on a blues form (in the key of Bb), where each third of it is treated as a miniature
three-phrase unit. That is, a short, rhythmically similar phrase is played in each of the first two measures, while the next two
measures contain a longer line that seems to answer the first two. And this whole plan repeats for both of the remaining four-
bar sections.

EXAMPLE 1

iy, TRACK 13

28

100 JAZZ LESSONS | GUITAR LESSON GOLDMINE

Throughout most of this example, an effort is also made to choose tones that fit the chord changes, though the very last
phrase is an exception, with some earthier blues scale material that “skates” through the chords, in a stylistically fitting way.
This last phrase also goes on a bit longer and gives a sense of conclusion by the end of the 12-bar form. Even if we've had the
same essential four-bar call-and-response pattern three times in a row, the third one of these seems to answer the first eight
measures altogether, neatly wrapping up one chorus of the blues.

In fact, the whole thing comes across like a bit of jazz-blues melody, and a similar approach has often been used to improvise
an actual “head” (melody) for a blues-form tune in the style. This is frequently done with a simpler “riff” that is played once
each for the first two four-bar chunks and answered in the last four measures, giving the whole thing some common ground
with the vocal blues structure described earlier,

Our next example makes use of another, more particular device within the framework of call and response, which is to jump
between higher and lower range of pitch on our instrument. This is especially reminiscent of the great tenor saxophonist
Eddie Harris, who, in his both funky and sophisticated manner, might leap in short order between extreme highs and lows.

EXAMPLE 2

FT

|

==

The concept is relatively simple, but it can actually provide a bit of a technical workout on the guitar if our lines of diverse
register are played close together in time. It forces us to quickly get our bearings in another place on the fretboard—either

across the neck, or along the neck, or both—to find the tones we mean to play, and to move our hand efficiently and
accurately for clean transitions.

This back-and-forth range-jumping idea can certainly be applied to a tune with more chord changes and a swinging rhythm,

besides the groovy two-chord vamp setting we have here. Still, it's probably a good effect to use sparingly, lest it become too
much of a gimmick in our playing.

Call and response was a prominent feature of the musical environment that helped to form the jazz style and continues to
appear throughout the tradition in compositions and in solo statements. See if you can recognize it when you hear it used in
more obvious or subtle ways; perhaps you'll desire to introduce such a “conversational” element to your own improvisation.

100 JAZZ LESSONS | GUITAR LESSON GOLDMINE 29

DOMINANT DECISIONS

When reading chord symbols from a lead sheet, we often see a progression outlined in very simple terms, without much
indication of chordal extensions (those odd numbers beyond 7) or alterations (sharp-this and flat-that). The reality of it is,
when looking at such a skeletal version of the song and its basic harmonies, we need to make interpretations about which
embellishments or substitutions we may use for each one—determinations of what each chord could actually be.

Nowhere is this truer than with dominant seven chords—the kind that may be named simply as “F7” or “B+7." With these, where
there is no further detail given, the biggest decision for each is whether it will be a natural or altered dominant. Beyond that,
there are still different sub-categories, as well as many options for extensions and voicings, but we'll focus here on some
particular ways to make this main distinction.

In some situations, the vagueness on the chord chart might be deliberate, because there really are different viable choices
we could make about the nature of the chord. Where the dominant seven is the V chord in a major ii-V-| movement, for
example, either natural or altered may work. This D7 in the key of G sounds fine as either D9 (natural, though extended) or
D7+9 (altered). For all our examples, the more generic “lead sheet” chord symbols will remain written on top of the staff:

Ultimately, you'll need to use your ear and pay attention to context to make good dominant seven decisions. But being aware
of the different types and their typical uses is an important first step.

THE ALTERED ANGLE

A common harmonic move in jazz Progressions is for a dominant seven chord to change from natural to altered before it
resolves in a V-I direction (like from D to G, or G to C, such that the root movement is down a perfect 5th). This gives it a sense
of extra angle, urgency, or momentum to get to the next chord.

This is especially true where the basic Progression has two measures’ worth of that dominant seven before it moves on, as is
the case repeatedly in this circle-of-fifths kind of chord progression:

G7 C7 F7

Altered dominant seven chords—the ones named as“7-flat-this” and “sharp-that"—can actually fall into various sub-categories,
depending on the exact combination of altered or unaltered tones they contain. Sometimes a piece of music calls for one of
these types in particular, and at other times, especially in a jazz situation, we are free to decide which it will be.

el Eve BLUES LICKS, JAZZ STYLE

As jazzers, we often focus on the ability to play melodic lines through specific chord changes, rather than just throwing in a
lot of wholesale blues licks or minor pentatonic runs, whether we're soloing on a blues or anything else. But some genuine
blues flavor is an important part of the tradition and a part of the expressive palette of jazz musicians. Guitarists such as Kenny
Burrell, Grant Green, and George Benson are particularly famous for their intertwining of blues material with the rest of the
jazz language, as are saxophonists like Stanley Turrentine and Eddie Harris.

Still, there are certain ways that jazz guitar players tend to delve into their bluesy side, usually without the more extreme
bending, sliding, shaking, or distorted screaming that can be found in the rock or blues genres (although a touch of vibrato is
nice). In playing jazz, we may want to play the blues, but without sounding as though we showed up at the wrong gig! So in
this lesson, I'm presenting a few ideas of the “blues lick” kind that are typical for the style—for when blues territory is visited
with a bebop perspective.

We'll start with a line that could be used on an F7 chord, or for many other places within a blues or other type of tune in the
key of F. It involves a whole-step slide, up and down, and tones of the good old F blues scale exclusively, right up until the
ending. Here we get a classic blues line wrap-up in the last four notes, moving through an E-natural (the major 7th of the key)

to come home to F.

Now we'll try one with a couple of bluesy double stops (two strings at once), as well as grace-note whole-step slides and a

Finally, we'll slow things down a bit with these phrases for the beginning of an F blues. Here we have a bluesy little
hammer-on/pull-off and some interesting things going on with triplet rhythm.

EXAMPLE 4

Bb7 F7

[— “reverse swing”—

8th-note triplets and swung 8ths can often blend together in the same flow, as is the case here, since each 8th-note pair is
much like a quarter and an 8th note together in a triplet (two thirds of the beat for the first note, one third for the second
note). But at the beginning of the second full measure, there's a figure that | sometimes think of as “reverse swing"—an 8th
and then a quarter note in a triplet, effectively giving us a short first half of the beat and a longer second half. Played with a
certain attack at slower tempos, it can convey quite a blues attitude!

Oftentimes when | hear a student make an early attempt at jazz improvisation, whether on a blues or some other kind of
tune, it seems they resort immediately to overt rock and blues devices of the guitar—big slides, bends, and shakes, and
lots of double stops—not to mention profuse blues scale material. And I'm not saying these things can't be done in the jazz
realm. In the big picture, all the expressive tools of our instrument are fair game, especially for a highly improvisational music
that encourages individuality and has taken in so many influences. Bending, shaking, and sliding, in addition to screaming
distortion, and any other imaginable effects, have all been successfully used at some point by jazz guitarists.

But here's the thing: in the case of these jazz novices, | get the feeling that they are hiding behind these habits, relying on
them for more obvious ways to sound wailin’ on the guitar, instead of dealing with the tonal and rhythmic language of a

music that is new to them. So | encourage them to put those things aside for the moment, study the vocabulary of the style,
and look for expressiveness in their choice of notes and rhythms.

In the long run, some blues feeling and content is indeed requisite for any musician in the style, and whether we reach far

into the blues bag or otherwise bring in techniques that are beyond the norm for jazz, the trick is to do it in a tasty way that
works for the music.

100 JAZZ LESSONS | GUITAR LESSON GOLDMINE 37

e-fe] FE -a:sa 3/4 COMPING

Tunes in 3/4 time are abundant in jazz and can groove with the best of the 4/4 repertoire. As chordal players of the music,
we want to be comfortable with rhythm in this meter and able to swing it like we would in 4. Most of what makes for good
comping applies to playing in 3 the same as it does to 4/4 time, and as usual it's important to get a feel for it through listening
and experience. But there are a few pointers and basic rhythms for swinging in 3/4 that we can check out for a decent start.

In jazz, there's frequently an emphasis on the “and” of beat 2 in a 3/4 measure. This is a point of time that, as the numbers go,
would be exactly halfway through the bar. (Note, however, that if we're in swung time, it'll be a little later in that 2nd beat,
giving the whole rhythm a certain lilt.) To get a sense for it, let’s try this chord progression, playing on beat one and the “and”
of 2 for each measure (and with a rest on beat 3, for this example):

that time.

Use your good judgment as to when to make use of this or any bass-and-chords kind of guitar rhythm. In a full band with a

Different time signatures yet certainly exist in jazz, 5/4 having been a famous one for quite a while, with others gaining in

popularity in the current era. 3/4 time, though, is a well-established aspect of the style, and with some listening, practice, and
awareness of its flow, you can be on your feet with chordal rhythms in this meter.

LESSON #19:

3/4 SOLOING

Historically, a sizable majority of the jazz repertoire has been in 4/4 time, and it's easy to wind up focused on this meter
exclusively in our practice. But the jazz waltz, or tune that swings in 3/4, has long been a significant part of the tradition, and
an aspiring musician in the style should get comfortable improvising in triple meter as well as “in 4”

This is in large part a matter of learning to feel the 3/4 swinging groove through hearing it performed by the pros and some
playing time of your own. But we may have done so much development of melodic ideas in 4/4 that we could ask ourselves,
on a case-by-case basis, “how would | play that line for the same chord progression in 3?”To explore this way of thinking, we'll
take a few 4/4 phrases and see how they might be adapted for a 3/4 jazz tune.

Here we have a very basic ii-V- idea in a major key, which works when the first two of these chords take up two beats apiece,

WR TECHNICAL TIPS FOR THE
sce SAME-FRET SYNDROME

It is in our nature as guitarists to play more than one note a time, taking advantage of our multiple strings even within a
melodic solo statement. Still, a jazz player needs the horn- or voice-like skill to cleanly execute single-note material without
unintended gaps or overlaps between tones. And one situation that can frequently pose a problem in this regard is when two
or more notes in a row must be played at the same fret on neighboring strings (or perhaps on distant strings).

The two main techniques | use to get through these spots, within written melodies or improvised ideas, are the finger roll and
finger substitution.

Let's look first at a couple of short phrases based on an Am7 arpeggio in the fifth position. At issue here are the notes we play
at the 5th fret on the G and B strings, likely both with the first (index) finger. If we barre those two strings, fretting them both
at once as in a chord, they will ring at the same time. And if we hop over from one string to the next with the tip of the same
finger, we might make it OK, but this is difficult, inefficient, and likely to give us an unwanted “breath” or little break in the line.

3 ee ee

What we need to do here, for distinct tones and a legato flow, is to roll with that first finger. In the first measure, place it on its
tip as usual for the note C on the third string, and roll off the tip onto the flatter part of the finger to fret the second string, This
takes some slow practice to get going smoothly, coordinating the release of the earlier note with the sounding of the next one.

For the second measure phrase, the finger roll needs to happen in reverse. When descending with this technique (heading to
a higher-numbered string and a lower pitch), we need to strategize a little more ahead of time: to play the note E here, place
the index finger more flatly on its underside at the 5th fret of the B string, such that you can roll up onto its tip on the G string.
Again, slow practice is the key, especially now for correct initial finger placement, as well as for the timing of things.

Next we try some more challenging finger roll situations: the index finger rolling down (in pitch) through three notes of C
minor in a classic, often-used jazz lick, and then a more modern-sounding, perfect 4th-based line, involving an upward roll
through four strings, as well as a smaller roll with the pinky.

EXAMPLE 1

TRACK 20

1

42

Cm7 D7sus4
a La) Fee
SSS] gas
+ Lis 8 j -}- 5 8 a a 5 —-

} * Bo . 2

For that longest roll, we may need to let our finger subtly shift across the strings to fret each note separately, trying not to
bend them out of pitch as we go. For any of these moves, let your finger be at a relaxed angle, maybe just a little off of the tip,
and avoid crushing your knuckle in towards the fretboard in the flatter position (for health’s sake). Your hand might need to

shift a bit too,

Our next example, with lines built around an Am7, and then a Dm/, calls for a different technique yet. All these notes are
playable within the fourth position for Am7, or the fifth position for Dm7, but if we stick with a one-finger-per-fret principle,
we wind up needing to do awkward reverse-rolls with our second and third fingers between the third and fourth strings.
More natural in these cases is to substitute a finger for the one that would've had double-duty at the same fret, placing our
fingers according to the fingering numbers shown here:

EXAMPLE 2

Finger substitution is generally done with the higher-numbered finger on the lower-numbered (higher-pitched) string and
may call to mind some familiar chord shapes.

ALTERNATE PICKING
BY RHYTHM

LESSON #21:

Alternate picking—the use of both down- and up-strokes with the pick for efficiency in playing single-note lines—is familiar
to many guitarists. In this lesson, we look at a particular use of it, especially for swung 8th-note melodies and solo ideas in jazz.

As opposed to a method that prescribes switching pick direction depending on which way we're moving to the next
string (with speed in mind), this approach involves a built-in groove in the picking hand, gained from allowing it to move
continuously with the beat. It's kind of a micro-version of common strumming patterns from other styles, in which the right
hand keeps moving down and up to the pulse of the music, catching all downbeats with a downstroke and all upbeats (the
“and” counts) with an upstroke, regardless of where there is space in between.

In jazz, we can apply this upstrokes-for-upbeats approach to our single-note playing, while still using mostly downstrokes for
typical comping (chording). To get this regularity of right hand movement going, we first practice our scales, or other steady-
stream-of-notes kind of exercises, with a completely consistent alternate picking pattern, like so:

a. &
= ===. J 4>- —— + Er — = > —*
a} SS SSS SSaaee: SoS SSS |
om VnV¥onV aA Y nmVonaVeAYVY 9 ¥ evn v Ym ¥ nmVoAaV»A
+~—— = —— ae — —f— _- — j
— = — — eee |
BS - — i ———L —49—8-—

Then we can try playing tunes and improv ideas—especially bop melodies, with their abundant 8th-note-based rhythms—
letting the momentum of the right hand continue, now with a swung lilt to its timing. A melodic pattern such as this one
(which is a syncopated version of the C major scale descending in 3rds) is a good place to start, making sure to hit each note
with the pick stroke indicated.

keep movin’ and groovin’ along the way.

i would not say that this alternate picking method is essential for everyone playing jazz guitar, at least not all the time. Its
applicability may depend on other elements in your technique. For some, “allowing the hand to move with the groove” may
mean allowing the thumb and forefinger to do this, if you use a more localized picking motion, or something else entirely
# you're not a pick player. Plus, there is a greater rhythmic flexibility in some modern forms of the music—not to mention
ballads and other situations in which we may have more time to approach each note. Technique can indeed color the
rhythmic feel of our playing, but ultimately it’s up to each of us to listen to the groove we hear in our mind’s ear, however we
Get it to come out on the instrument.

100 JAZZ LESSONS | GUITAR LESSON GOLDMINE 45

MD STRAIGHT-4
patie STRAIGHT AHEAD

Straight-4, or “four-to-the-bar” chording is a great part of the jazz guitar tradition. It is also sometimes referred to as “Freddie
Green style,’ after its most revered practitioner, who for half a century anchored the rhythm section of the Count Basie
Orchestra with his “chunk chunk” groove.

It is essentially what the name implies: quarter-note chords played on each beat of a 4/4 measure. This would seem simple
enough, but it is a particular skill to do it well and make it swing (and help the band to swing with you).

Rhythmically, it is not just a continual blur of sustained quarter notes, each running right into the next. There needs to be just
a little release between each chord (letting it be muted by lifting the pressure of your left hand). But it also should not sound
like a march, with a short chopping sound on each beat due to too much release. You could think of the timing of this release

as having to do with the swung-ness of the quarter-note rhythm (or better yet, just listen to some Freddie Green and try to get
a feel for it).

As for chord voicings, this is usually a good place to be moderate or minimal in size, and some small root-3rd-and-7th kind of

chord shapes can work very nicely for the texture. Here we'll use these three-string chords to try playing through a standard
progression in straight-4 rhythm:

EXAMPLE 1

Here are a few more things to keep in mind about straight-4 chording:

» Asis the case with a lot of jazz chording, we need a unified sound for each chord (the tones sounding together and
not like an arpeggiated strum), with a pick attack that is firm enough, but not harsh. Remember, “chunk chunk" not
“brrrap brrrap.”

) The quarter-note groove is quite regular—not a “long-short-long-short,” staccato-on-beats-2-and-4 treatment of the
chords that can imply an old-school two-feel.

) It works best on acoustic, but we can render it OK on electrified instruments with careful tone and touch. Freddie
Green himself used an unamplified archtop to play with a whole big band.

) It has particular functions in the music and generally does not work where more variable comping is called for in a
modern small jazz group (including in the bop genre). Depending on the situation, we might find use for it in a big
band, a retro-styled performance, or very small settings like a duo, where guitar alone accompanies a horn, voice,
piano, or another guitar. Or perhaps we can use it for backing a bass solo.

) Keep the volume low. “Felt more than heard" is the classic description of its role in the swing-era big band, and in
other scenarios it could be more obnoxious than tasty if we play it on top of our bandmates, rather than behind them.
You might afford a little more sound sometimes—like in a guitar/trumpet duo—but don't bury that bass soloist!

The straight-4 approach to chords is an important study for the jazz guitarist, to be in touch with the tradition, and to have
this texture available for the right setting. It’s also a great way to solidify the chords to a tune and our sense of time along
with it.

100 JAZZ LESSONS | GUITAR LESSON GOLDMINE 47

48

TWO-NOTE
CHORDAL SHELLS

LESSON #23:

For the times that less would be more in our choice of chord shapes, movable shell voicings can be a handy and tasteful
option. Basically, it’s the root, 3rd, and 7th of a chord that really define its main type, and we're talking about breaking various
kinds of chords down to these essential tones (and then leaving off the root, to boot).

What we have left are very compact, easy-to-play, and flexible little two-string voicings that can still serve to outline a chord
progression, especially when put together with the walking line of a bassist. Because they are so pared down, there is less
concern when we use them whether somebody else in the band is playing a+5th, a natural versus raised 9th, etc. on any given
chord—those members are simply not there!

But also because these voicings are so spare, the same small shape on the fretboard will actually be the 3rd-and-7th shell of
more than one chord, so they may be tougher to keep track of than to execute. In this lesson, we'll take a look at how some
larger, more familiar chord forms can be trimmed down to reveal the shell shapes, which is helpful for locating them on the
instrument. We'll also hear how a chord progression can be rendered with minimal range of sound and movement through
their use.

Here are some of these bigger-to-smaller “evolutions” for basic chord types:

Bb7, big barre Bb7, better size for jazz 3-note Bb7 Bb7, 2-note shell
aa, x x x xx MX xx
ofr 6fr 6fr 6tr
Bbm7, big barre Bbm7, better size for jazz 3-note Bbm7 Bbm7, 2-note shell
xx eso xXx xx
$9 %6fr fr 1 6fr 4 6fr
|
Bbmaj7, basic voicing 3-note Bbmaj? Bbmaj?, 2-note shell
x xx x
1 6fr 6fr Tfr
E7, basic voicing 3-note E7 E7, 2-note shell
x x x xx x.

a i
Em7, basic voicing 3-note Em7 Em/7, 2-note shell
XS x ae XX Xx

i

x x 2 x xx %. 5
Sir 6fr 1 6fr 6fr
|

The notes that are the 3rd and 7th for any chord can be found in all areas of the fretboard, but for now we'll stick with these
shell voicings that lie on the middle two strings only. This is both technically and sonically convenient, as it puts us in a mellow
midrange that rings clearly enough while blending nicely with others in the band. Please be aware—we don't automatically
get the right notes for these by taking any chord shape and cutting it down to the middle strings. In the examples above,
that's where the needed tones happen to be.

100 JAZZ LESSONS | GUITAR LESSON GOLDMINE

Here are similar shells for a few other basic chords, without the same derivation process. See if you can relate them to larger
voicings that you know. For any “six” type of chord, we're actually using its 3rd and 6th tones:

Bb6 Bbm6 E6 Em6 Bb°7 or Db°7 or E°7 or G°7
Sfr Str Sfr fr fr

Remember that chords with -5,""9;’ etc. will just use the shells of the same chord types without the alterations, since the 3rd
and 7th don't change (and if you come across a half-diminished seven, it’s effectively the same as minor seven with abSth).

Notice that, for all these different chords, we have used only three shapes of shell voicing on the fretboard! Among all the
overlapping forms, we could also see that the B>7 and E7 examples give us the exact same thing for their shells. They are

tritone substitutes of one another, and this is one place where they “meet” Although for B:7, the chordal 7th is on the bottom
and the 3rd is on top, and vice versa for E7.

Ble) E7- a JUMPING TRACKS

In this lesson, we look at how some very typical patterned scale practice can be applied to changing chords in a process that
might serve simply as further exercise, but may also yield musical results that we consider “keepers” for our improvisational
approach.

First, let's consider a couple of common ways that jazz musicians play their scales in patterns in order to gain technical control
beyond the straight-up-and-down method. For the purpose of this lesson, we'll label these “Pattern 1” and “Pattern 2," although
there are many possible variations of this scalar practice regimen.

In Pattern 1, we play through the scale in groups of four consecutive descending notes, starting with the tonic, and doing the
same thing from each next tone downwards. Here's how it looks on a C major scale, within a limited range:

Pattern |

SSS ==> === 2

7 = i —s

542 42 2 a
z 5 53 $—o—2 53 fee 2

Realize that we could certainly go further with this pattern or play it in reverse, in different ways—moving the group of
descending notes up through the scale or using groups of four ascending notes, etc. Many permutations are possible,
including the use of smaller or larger note-groups, and you could certainly try coming up with some of these on your own.

This next pattern is one that involves a change of direction within each grouping, and it could be described as “up one step in
the scale, up one more step in the scale, and back down a 3rd interval to the starting note,’ with that whole unit moving up
stepwise through the scale. Notice the word “step” here refers to the distance to the next note in the scale—not necessarily a
whole step. Here's how it sounds on C major:

Pattern 2

It can be plenty enough practice material, for starters, to play one-at-a-time through the various scales in our repertoire,
using different up-and-down patterns such as these. But here's the clincher of this lesson, as it pertains to soloing on changes:
we'll now try to maintain one of these patterns while shifting to different scales along the way to reflect a particular chord
progression.

We'll start with a progression that goes back and forth between two major chords (the major seven #11, to be exact), a half step
apart, changing every measure. Here are the scales we'll need:

C Lydian for Cmaj7# 1 = D> Lydian for Domaj7#tt

52

Ble) FE ys-a “THE LICK”

A particular YouTube video, one that went viral in the jazz world, featured a ridiculously varied assortment of performers all
playing one little seven-note phrase dubbed “the lick.” It included famous jazz musicians, rock and dance bands, and solo and
orchestral classical performers. It was put together for the humor of it, but it’s probably no accident that this simple melodic
cell is so widespread, particularly in the language of jazz.

In this lesson we'll take a look at “the lick” itself—not just to play what everyone else does or because it’s funny—but because
it is a classic example of a versatile musical idea with many applications, especially for improvisation. Here it is, basically, in A
minor:

interval pattern, can fit into many scales, chords, and situations, often in more than one way.

THE EXTENSION DIMENSION

The odd-numbered members of a chord beyond the 7th are called its extensions, and focusing on them as a soloist can add
a colorful dimension’to your playing. In this lesson, we'll try an approach to playing on the upper extensions of a couple
fundamental chord types, along the way taking a look at the scalar origin of each to be clear on what those tones are.

Let's start with the minor seven chord, using the Dorian scale to derive the full gamut of its tones (this works better than the
natural minor scale by the time we get to the chordal 13th, which will be determined by the scalar 6th). Here the tones are
numbered for the scale with the idea that we are repeating at the octave, while when enumerating chord tones we keep
counting upwards. This is how the scale’s 6th becomes the chord’s 13th, etc., and how Dm/7, extended, becomes Dm13.

D Dorian

Realize that the chordal arpeggios resulting from this process are more like lists of chordal members, while in the chord forms
we actually play (our chord voicings), they have usually been reordered, often with non-essential tones omitted.

It's good to know, for many purposes, what all the tones of the chords we play really are and to practice them in arpeggio form
in different places. But beyond this, we can look at what other chords are found within the arpeggio of the chord in question.
There will be several possibilities, but let's note first that from the 5th on up in this Dm13 arpeggio, we have exactly the tones of
Am9—A, C, E, G, and B (the further up we go, the more we're looking at a chord that sits in the extensions of the original one).

Now we can test out the implications for improv purposes on a Dm7 chord. We'll do this by trying something purely within Am9

It's also possible to play on the dominant chord extensions that come from a regular Mixolydian scale, which gives us a
normal minor seven chord built on the 5th of the dominant seven—a very Wes Montgomery kind of sound. The chordal 11th
is then the non-raised 4th of the scale, which can clash with the 3rd, but within that arpeggio from the Sth it can imply the
sound of a suspended chord, or of a ii-V chord pair (as if, in this case, Dm7 were the ii and G7 were the V).

For major seven chords, a Lydian scale will give us the fully extended major thirteen 211, here again avoiding the clashing
4th/11th that was in the more obvious scale (major). We can find a major nine arpeggio from its Sth.

We need the right scale for each chord, of course, and the system may seem less clear for certain chord types, especially
those that have been altered after the fact (for altered dominants, consider where all those sharped and flatted tones may be,
whether or not they fit neatly into new arpeggios). But experiments in staying up among the extensions, wherever we can
find them, can yield interesting new possibilities for our improvisation.

100 JAZZ LESSONS | GUITAR LESSON GOLDMINE 55

Ble) Eye MAJOR SEVEN #11

The major seven chord with a #11th tone is an interesting animal among jazz chords. It has a fresh, oblique kind of sound, often
calling to mind the “s5th” accent of bebop harmony. It can be interjected as a surprise coloration where a more normal major
chord is expected, but could also be specifically called for in a composition, maybe harmonizing that raised 11th note in the
melody or used in a modal setting.

This chord is very related to the Lydian scale, equivalent to a major scale with a raised 4th degree, which could be used to
generate the chord when played upwards in 3rd intervals:

C Lydian Cmaj7#1t Arpeggio
R Id Sth 7th wh lith = 13th

T 2nd ed Ath Sth 6th Tth T Ind 3rd dh Sth 6th 7th

o. a ee 174 |

oo se ?

Every tone of this scale can thus be part of the chord, harmoniously. This doesn’t work so easily from the tonic of the major
scale, given the potential clash between its 3rd and 4th tones.

Notice that the 11th tone is enharmonically equivalent to ab5th, which means same pitch, different name in theory (F# vs. G,
in this case). As you see, it comes from the #4th of the scale, which we could think of as either the 4th degree naturally found
in Lydian, or a raised 4th from a major scale (so in case you ever come across the designation “maj7+5,” you'll have an idea what
is meant).

Here are some movable shapes for major 411 chords on the guitar. We'll include maj9#11 voicings, as well as another variety
that is technically not even a major seven chord, though it is part of the major family—the 6/9 chord with a #11th on it—which
is a very pretty option that we don't want to leave out. We'll stick with C as the root for our chord shapes and soloing examples
for clarity. It can all be transposed later on!

In a band situation, use your ear if you're going to throw one of these in where not expected! It can add interest to the tune,

include the regular 5th in the chord, these common guitar voicings leave it out for simplicity’s sake (and you know we're not
sustaining all seven members of the extended chord together on a standard guitar anyway!).

Ble) E74: BOSSA RHYTHM

Various Latin American rhythms have become an integral part of the jazz tradition, not least among them the Brazilian bossa
nova. Many songs of this relaxed and melodic style are fixtures in the repertoire, and jazz musicians have freely adapted other
tunes to be played with a bossa rhythm, as well as writing their own bossa-flavored compositions. We guitarists in particular
would do well to have at least some grounding in the genre, since our instrument has a lot to do with its fundamental sound.

The bossa nova beat is based on even 8th notes, as opposed to the usual swing 8ths of straight-ahead jazz, although it may
sometimes have a bit of a swung lilt to it—especially in the hands of a bop-styled musician! As with much of Latin jazz, it is
sometimes played more authentically, and sometimes highly modified.

Bossa nova guitar in its original form involves fingerstyle chording (traditionally on nylon strings) in a way that was developed
and popularized by Jodo Gilberto. A chordal groove like this one would be typical, with the right-hand thumb sounding the
bass notes, and the fingers playing and muting the rest of the chord:

A key factor in getting a good feel for bossa nova rhythm—chordal or melodic, authentic or jazzified—is of course listening

Stan Getz and the tradition’s greatest composer, Antonio Carlos Jobim), Wes Montgomery soloing on “Canadian Sunset,”
or trumpeter Lee Morgan's beautiful jazz bossa “Ceora.” From Brazilian to hard-bop and beyond, there’s much to enjoy and
explore as you try your hand at this beautiful side of jazz.

THE OCTAVES TEXTURE

The technique of playing melodic lines in octaves, doubling each note with the same note an octave away, is a common device
used by jazz guitarists to beef up their sound and add textural variety. Most famously associated with Wes Montgomery, it’s
been part of the palette for artists as diverse as Wes, Django Reinhardt, and John Scofield, each making it fit their own unique
style.

The handiest way to play these octaves is with their notes two strings away from each other (with one inactive string in
between) and also two or three frets apart, depending which strings we're on. The natural fingering for this formation is with
the index finger on the bottom note and the third finger on the top note if it’s on the D or G string (two frets higher), or the
fourth finger if it’s on the B or E string (three frets higher). The string in between is muted by the index finger (at least if you're
a pick or thumb player), and we'll use all downstrokes for starters.

With this approach to the fingering, let’s try a B major scale in the octaves texture (and we'll go past one octave of the scale—
don't get the uses of the word mixed up!):

>
xs
oxe | | |
LLL

With reasonable knowledge of the fretboard and some practice, it can be relatively easy to see where these octave shapes lie,
but it’s another thing to execute them cleanly in licks or melodies. This texture presents a different technical challenge than
single-note lines, since it requires us to scoot around the guitar with much less variation in finger-usage for the left hand.
Oftentimes, where staying close to one position would normally be good for efficient fingering, we may want to move more
vertically along the neck (between higher and lower frets) for ergonomic octaves.

This is most apparent when we try playing in octaves for melodic ideas that jump around, more than in step-by-step scalar
motion, or for chordal arpeggios, which move in 3rd intervals. To check out how this can work, we'll contrast two different
fingerings (or positionings, really) for an Am11 arpeggio, shown in the tablature below:

Amt! Arpeggio (Option 1) AmI1 Arpeggio (Option 2)

The first of these options stays closer to one position and follows a shape that works well for the top notes if they are played
alone. The second one takes a more vertical route that minimizes the back-and-forth jumping of the index finger for the
bottom notes and is less jagged altogether. In this method of fingering octaves, the ring finger and pinky usually switch off
a bit as we move between string sets, while the index finger is the constant. Considering how it will need to move can be
helpful when working out how to play an octave run.

100 JAZZ LESSONS | GUITAR LESSON GOLDMINE
To work this technique into your playing, try some of your favorite tunes or ideas in octaves (you could start with slower-
moving things) and see what works well for you in terms of placement on the fretboard. Besides what's smoothest for the
hand and fingers, there's also the consideration of where you might quickly find the phrase on the guitar, if you were in mid-
flight on an improvised solo.

Much of what applies to the kind of melodic ideas we want to play is the same for octaves as for single-note material
generally. Here are a few lines that would sound fine with normal one-string-at-a-time delivery, but come across nicely with
the octave treatment. Our first example is at the beginning of a blues progression in Bb:

Octaves can also be used for some one-note colorations behind a melody or rhythmic statements in a solo that would fall flat
with ordinary single-string playing. As much as they are often used deliberately to evoke the sound of Wes, we can do like
jazz guitarists have done for generations and make the octaves texture part of our own musical toolbox.

MINOR SHAPE MADNESS

Whether studying extended chordal arpeggios, the harmonic content of scales, or chord shapes on the fretboard, we can
quickly realize how much overlap of note material there is, even between very different-sounding chord types. Our system of
harmony is full of chords found within chords, and this has big implications for an improviser.

The common ground between various harmonies is especially evident in certain minor-sounding formations, viewed either in
terms of scale tones or chordal members.

Listen to, and take a look at, this frequently heard melodic idea in the jazz language, as it compares to the forms of four
different chords with which it fits perfectly (all shown here with some potential variation, to help show their relationship to the
line). We'll call it “Minor Shape 1” for the purpose of this lesson:

Minor Shape | b
-
4 = = £ =be- Pe z | Bbm6 (or §) Eb9 (or 13) AT (or #9) Gm7b5 (or Gm] 1b5)
CC ——— FFA str RAS ¢ Str Sfr
8 6 _— — f + Hott
i — = —+——— 2 1333 21333 1 2333 1222
IB = = (4) (4) (4) (4)

For use of this little phrase in multiple situations, it is handy to be aware of exactly how it relates to each chord. We could say,
for example, that it starts on the 9th of a minor chord, the 13th of a natural dominant, the #9th of an altered dominant, or the
lith (seems like an added 4th) of a minor seven +5 chord (could be called half-diminished).

Each of these chords, as well as the line itself, contains a minor triad—a B> minor triad in this case—where we barre the top
three strings for all of these voicings. The second, third, and fourth notes of Minor Shape 1 make up this same unit. The only
other tone actually found in this melodic phrase is the one it starts with: the chordal 9th or scalar 2nd (relative to B> minor).
The final two notes of the line are just a repeat of the first two an octave lower.

The scalar common ground between these chords, as they are, is basically the Bs melodic minor scale. If we start from the note
A, this becomes the A altered (or super Locrian) scale, and starting from B; produces the E> Lydian dominant scale.

B> Melodic Minor

—————————

e

Notice that the tones of our versatile soloing idea are just the 1st, 2nd, 3rd, and 5th degrees of this scale. The first five tones of
the minor scale altogether yield numerous melodic possibilities that are flexible in the same way and very often used in jazz
improvisation. Notice too that this part of the scale will stay the same through different versions of minor, or the similar Dorian
scale (if our B minor chord were a minor seven, we'd probably look to B> Dorian instead, and the related G Locrian may be
preferred for the Gm7+5).

100 JAZZ LESSONS | GUITAR LESSON GOLDMINE

Ultra-mobile minor melodic cells like these can be found in the improvisation of many players—sometimes even in smaller
bits and pieces:

Minor Shape 2 Minor Shape 3 Minor Shape 4 Minor Shape 5

Here as well, we can make note of how each line is connected to the chords we may wish to play it over. Those that begin

with the tonic B will fit a Bb minor chord starting on its root, £9 starting on its Sth, A7+9 starting on its+9th, or Gm715 starting
on its 3rd.

To realize just how versatile these minor ideas are, check out how this standard chord progression can be played on using

mostly just Minor Shapes 1 and 2, or fragments of either one (now transposed to various places). Note we're using a one-flat
key signature now, for the key of D minor:

THE CHROMATIC
CONNECTION

It can be fun and interesting to include some chromatic fret-by-fret motion in our jazz improv lines, stepping outside of regular
scales, creating a different kind of flow, and sometimes invoking a more modern sound. If we wish to maintain relevance to
the chord or chord changes along the way, it’s a good idea to be clear on where the chord tones are and seek to connect them
or target them with our half-step movement.

We'll take a look here at a few examples of how this may work in specific situations. You may be able to compare these linear
ideas to familiar chord shapes to see how they relate. But for each of them, I'll first show where the pertinent chord tones lie
in a close-by area of the fretboard, almost in the form of the official arpeggios (which are built in 3rds). In doing so, I'll focus
mainly on the notes we'll want to spot in the melodic example at hand, including 9ths and 11ths. The “R” stands for “root,” by
the way!

Here's one for a C7 chord:

EXAMPLE 1

jy, TRACK 31

It starts off on D, the natural 9th for C7, connecting it by half step to the root, then moving similarly down from the 7th to
the Sth (B+ to G) and further downwards to create a chromatic enclosure of the 3rd (circling around E before landing on it). It
finishes with another pair of descending half steps to connect root to 7th, and by the time it’s done, all twelve tones of the
chromatic scale have been used, while the sound is still very C7! Notice that any note placed on beats 1 or 3 here is a basic
chord tone (the root, 5th, and 3rd respectively).

How to move by half steps on the guitar is most obvious along one string (fret-by-fret), but we do of course need to know

some chord tones on strong beats, connects them chromatically, and uses an enclosure of the 3rd. You could try this same line
on Am/7 as well, given its strong connection to Cmaj7!

Next, here's an idea for a ii-V-i in Bb minor (though it seems to start off a lot like our first example). This one sounds best with
a little speed to it:

Sa

———

=

z
ci

‘3 —__————_—§

F

As the second measure of this phrase begins, the half-step descending tones on the B string start to bounce off of other notes
in between, on the G string, in a kind of pattern reminiscent of the hip modern stylings of Pat Metheny or John Abercrombie.
Here it lends a brief “outness” to the line, in particular because the note G landing right on beat 1 doesn’t really fit with the
F76985 chord, and neither does the E that follows. But on the next beat, they move down to G and E;, rejoining the chord
tones. All this works as part of a chromatic flow targeting the F on beat 3, from which we continue in a more chord-shaped
kind of way through our altered F7 and into Bsm7.

Half-step motion is found abundantly in the melodic material of jazz, sometimes sounding very “inside"—normal, perhaps
even old-school—and sometimes bringing us into more contemporary territory, with a bit of surprise to the sound, even
while staying close to the changes. It can be also used in heading “outside,” for a kind of playing that is less concerned with
chord progressions altogether. Still, where relating to harmony is a goal, we can be better prepared for chromatic adventures
by mapping out our chord tones well and keeping their sound and location in mind as we construct our lines.

MB SUSPENDED DISBELIEF
Soi (FUN WITH SUS CHORDS)

The suspended four chord is common in popular music as well as jazz, and can be built based on either a major triad or
dominant seven chord. It involves using a scalar natural 4th degree instead of the major 3rd that is usually there in these chord
types (that is, F instead of E if it's C major or C7). This 4th tone might, as the name implies, be “hanging” there with a tendency
to move back down to the 3rd.

In jazz, we're pretty much talking about the 7sus4, and the chord is more likely than in other styles to stay as-is (not reverting
to the use of the major 3rd). It may be played as an interesting substitute for a regular dominant seven, as a colorful sound for
its own sake, or to harmonize that chordal 4th tone when it is sustained in a melody.

Here are a few basic movable shapes for the 7sus4, each preceded by the regular dominant seven voicing to which it relates
(you can see where the chordal 3rd originally was):

Ab7 Ab7sus4 C7 C7sus4 Bb7 Bb7sus4 F7 F7sus4
x<_~a x nx ~~ .*
af 4ir $4fr 3fr : 3fr + Tfr Str 6fr 6fr
+ —» | —> Here > >
I ; | T
131211 131411 1314 1314 2314 1112 231 341

There are two main things to keep in mind that can help us find more and more variations of the dominant seven with a
suspended 4th:

Extended dominant seven chords—that is, nine and thirteen chords, mainly the natural type—can also be made into sus4
chords by replacing the chordal 3rd with the 4th tone (the natural 11th of a dominant chord winds up being the same note as
our 4th).

The 7sus4 chord is very much like a minor seven chord built on its 5th, including the possible extensions of the 9th or 11th
on that minor seven chord (its 11th actually brings us back to the root of the sus chord, while its 13th creates something else
again, which we'll discuss later).

Exploring the possibilities in either of these ways can often lead to the same thing, for specific voicings. As an example, let's
see how we might arrive at this D9sus4 voicing from two different angles.

D9 D9sus4 Am7 Am7/D (or D9sus4)
x 3 Fo
f Afr Str ¢ SSfr = (SEF FeStr
t —> or —
21333 Hil 2 3333 Hn

Now considering the dominant nine or thirteen shapes you may know, if you also know where that chordal 3rd is, you have
some place to look for dominant sus4 options. And so too, if you think about what you know for minor seven, nine, or eleven
shapes—use the right tone as the root (a perfect Sth below or perfect 4th above the root of the minor seven), and you're in
suspended city! (Make sure it’s a real minor seven type of chord though and not a minor six chord or minor with a major 7th.)

But aside from all the figuring-out, here are some of the nifty extended sus4 voicings that can be derived in those ways (some
of them are pretty stretchy—don't get hung up on the toughest ones at the moment):

Bb9sus4 —-Bb9sus4. Ss Bb 13sus4 *BbI3sus4. ~—-- Eb9sus4 © *Eb13sus4. =~ Eb 13sus4 ~—E 13sus4

could be called a 7sus4(add3), but is sometimes referred to as a 7add4. Here are a couple of voicings | like, with the 3rd up on
top:

If we refer to the minor seven chord built on the Sth of a 7sus4, making it a minor thirteen chord will also re-introduce that
3rd tone—that is, Cm13/F = F7sus4(add3). It’s a really cool sound, but use discretion if you want to try it where a 7sus4 is
specifically called for to avoid an unwanted clash.

To hear how these suspended sounds can lend a new flavor to an old form, let’s put them to use for a sus chord subbed-out

chords, and a surprise harmony at the turnaround. Some particular voicing options will be shown above the measures in
which | use them, and I'll sometimes switch off between a couple of these for each main chord as | comp (maybe leaving off
a bass note or two as well).

MB HIGH SUSPENSE
maaaiihese (SOLOING ON SUS CHORDS)

improvising over a dominant seven chord with a suspended 4th is a lot like playing on regular, unaltered dominant sevens,
but it's good for a soloist to know the distinction between the two and to be aware of some helpful concepts for melodically
digging into the sus4 sound.

The 7sus4 chord basically uses the natural 4th tone of its scale (the Mixolydian, or dominant scale) in place of the major 3rd
that we'd normally expect. For example, a G7sus4 will contain the note C instead of B, as in plain old G7. Let's look at just a few
chord shapes for reference; we'll stick with G as the root within this lesson:

Big barre Smaller, more jazz- Same voicing,
version: friendly version: higher up:
G7sus4 G7sus4 G7sus4
x ~~  s
Sfr 10fr

sie

t °
131411 1324 1314

Mixolydian may be the most obvious scale to turn to for dominant seven improv material, and we can use it for the 7sus4 as
well. When playing over the regular dominant seven, we need to be careful how we land on the scale's 4th degree because
of its potential clash with the chordal 3rd. We might even be inspired to think of Mixolydian with a raised 4th instead (Lydian
dominant), which avoids this clash. But that is no issue with the 7sus4! That 4th tone is right there in the chord, and now we
may even have to be cautious in how we involve the scalar 3rd. Still, the full Mixolydian scale can work as a source of melodic
tones for this setting.

Here’s a refresher on the scale, followed by an example of how its tones can sound on G7sus4. In this instance, both the 3rd
and 4th (the notes B and C, respectively) are certainly included in the line, but, to consider a finer point, the 4th is emphasized
in a way that would not sit well against a regular G7. It does, however, bring out the suspended sound nicely:
