} Measure 1: All the notes of this bar can be related to the B> diminished scale. Do you know it? If not, you'd better learn

This jazz theory lesson should make you realize the need to study the basic rules of music. All the descriptions are drawn
from knowing intervals, scales, keys, etc. We take the basics and scramble them up in jazz. This art form might not be for
everybody, and I'm quoting Cecil Taylor when | say that.

100 JAZZ LESSONS | GUITAR LESSON GOLDMINE 145
TRAIN WRECK DISSONANCE

Whenever | hear a strange chord sound and I'm in the middle of a jazz improvisation, | ask myself, “Are you going to try and
figure out exactly what the harmony is for that chord, or are you going to spontaneously play something that sounds as if
you know what you're doing?” For years, I've been reading books and watching instructional videos and still can’t figure out
how anyone could possibly think quickly enough to play a perfectly harmonizing harmonic passage over an altered chord
spontaneously when they're improvising at a fast tempo.

Let’s say you're at a jam session and you're busy blowing some of your best riffs. Suddenly, the piano player—and it's usually
a piano player—throws in a strange sounding chord; if you're not sure what the chord is, what are you going to play? Are
you going to ask yourself, “Now is that a G13+5, G7#9, or a G13+5+9 chord?" A puritanical jazz musician might concern himself
with that, but | wouldn't. A great jazz teacher, Mr. Bob Conti, told me, “Play whatever you want.’ He also told me he didn't care
what the scales were called either as long as it sounds good. I'll attempt to explain these lines one bar at a time. What sort of
impression do these altered chords make when you hear them?

CHROMATIC
ASCENDING RIFFS

You can take any riff and repeat it chromatically up the fretboard a half step (one fret) at a time. | got this idea by watching
fusion players like John Abercrombie and John Scofield. The trick is to resolve the line by ending on notes that lie firmly in key.
You can target notes up the neck and end up there. In this first example, | begin and end on notes that are in the key of C.

Ble) E74 DIMINISHED FLUENCY

In studying music, we are taught that certain scales harmonize with certain chords. Certain liberties can be taken with this
philosophy, which leaves more room for experimenting. My first jazz teacher taught me to apply the diminished scale to any
chord you could construct using these notes. A C diminished scale contains C-Db—E)-E-F#-G-A-B», or the 149-29 (or b3)-3-45
(or #4)-5-6-47. We can construct a basic C7 chord using the 1, 3, 5, and+7 of this scale. If we stick to the theory that we can play
the scale over any chord we created from it, then the C diminished scale will work over the C7 chord. There may be some that
would argue with that, but this is jazz we're talking about, and we can make our own rules. We're not interested in making a
pop recording. We want to stretch any limitations and break free into new realms of harmony and melody. At least, that’s what
we should set out to do. If this sound is new to you, it may take some time to get used to it.

DIMINISHED CHORD?
HARMONIC MINOR

People may tend to just use a diminished scale when it comes to playing over a diminished chord. The main reason for
studying harmony is to find as many options as you can. Since a diminished chord can be found in the harmonic minor scale,
we can also use the harmonic minor scale over the diminished chord. Here's a breakdown of triads in the A harmonic minor
scale:

i iie biH+ iv Vv VI viie
Am Be C+ Dm E F Ge

The harmonic minor sound has a sweet melodic quality and is very effective in jazz.

If we analyze the diminished chord, we find that it repeats itself every three half steps. In other words, B, D, F, and G# are
all three half steps (or frets) apart, and the chords are built from the same four notes. In these examples, both the B and G#
diminished chords could be thought of as the same chord (B° = B-D-F-G# and Ge = G¢-B-D-F). Both chords have the same
notes; they're just in a different order. Both are found in the A harmonic minor scale.

When | was first trying to become a jazz guitarist, and I'm still trying, | was so determined to get good that | practically only
played that diminished scale for a while. | thought of myself as the next hot jazz man on the scene and was prepared and
ready to show everybody what | could do. One day | was invited to jam with a notable pianist who had just recently released
a record. During our rehearsal/jam session, he said it was important that musicians should find their own style, but it sounded
as though he was mainly directing that talk toward himself. | thought that’s exactly what | was doing in my musical search
and rampage with the diminished scale. After hours of playing, | left the jam-session and felt certain | was on the right track
and would eventually end up playing with all the jazz greats some day. Not long after that, | had a conversation with a friend
of mine who was also friends with that same pianist. During our little chat, it was brought to everyone's attention that | never
played one straight note during the entire jam session. At first | thought | was following the “rules of jazz” by going as far
outside the lines as | could. Evidently to them, nothing | played was worth listening to, and all | did was play a lot of tasteless
diminished scales. The point of the story is you have to be tastefulvand discerning. Use the diminished scale here and there to
color the music, but add it to all the other beautiful sounds in the music library.

This ability to write and play over changes in jazz requires a lot of study and hard work. I've never once heard any musician
say, “Playing over changes is easy, and anyone can learn to do it.” If anything, it’s hard work that eventually makes it easier.
You have to listen to a lot of music and study the best players. Guitarists should also listen to pianists like Bill Evans and Oscar
Peterson. They command their instruments and play over changes like no one else. Listen to great horn players like John
Coltrane and Miles Davis too. Try and break away from clichéd guitar riffs.

LESSON #78:

DOMINANT CHORD?
MELODIC MINOR

For the blues musician that wants to break into jazz, this is probably the most exciting stage. To hear jazzy sounding lines in
the blues is a desire that many musicians have. Not all guitarists feel that this should be done, and from the purist's standpoint,
it shouldn't. You either stick to the blues riffs, or you don’t. However, we're talking about jazz here, and jazz is the direction
we're heading in.

The A melodic minor scale is simply the A major scale with a flat or minor 3rd: 1-2-+43-4-5-6-7. The same harmonic applications
apply to the melodic minor scale. Let’s look at a harmonized A melodic minor scale in seventh chords:

i ii” oill+ IV Vv vi vii”
Am(maj7) Bm715 Cmaj7+ D7 E7 Fem7+5 Gim7)5

Remember, these chords were created from the A melodic minor scale.

Here we're using some standard changes found in practically all styles of blues, jazz, and pop music to demonstrate the

minor scale." Over the top of the E7 and D7 chords, I'm only using the A melodic minor scale.

EXAMPLE 4

The second line over the A7 chord is a blues riff constructed using the A Mixolydian scale. It also has a blue note (the 29th or
53rd) at the 8th fret on the high E string. In blues theory, there are rules that let you use minor sounding notes (-3rd, b5th) over
a dominant chord to give the music that bluesy sound. The same train of thought should be used in jazz as well. Most people
feel that the best jazz musicians are also excellent blues players. Many educational halls of learning say that a jazz musician
should start by studying the blues, and more often than not, that proves to be true.

MELODIC MINOR =
SUPER LOCRIAN

The melodic minor scale, sometimes called the jazz minor, has its prominent position in jazz, and many musicians rely on its
sound for improvising or writing lines over altered chords. The super Locrian mode for G is G-Ab~-Be-G.-D>-B-F. Numerically,
that looks like 1+2-43-3-45-46-47. It basically has all the altered notes: >}5, 25, 59, and #9. It’s also important to note the 1, 3, and
»7, which create a dominant seven chord without the perfect 5th tone of the scale. The example below is to show you that the
A» melodic minor scale contains the same notes as the G super Locrian scale. We could say that the G super Locrian scale is the
seventh mode of Ab melodic minor. Most musicians think of the super Locrian scale as a melodic minor scale that's built a half
step higher than the root of the altered dominant chord over which you're playing.

Moderately fast Moderately fast
G Super Locrian Ab Melodic Minor

used to the sound of altered chords and then the impression the line makes on your ears. Eventually you want to be as familiar
with this sound as that of any other major or minor scale.

ele) Et: HALF STEP NEIGHBORS

The term “half step neighbors” refers to all the notes that are one fret (or a half step) above or below any note in any scale
you're playing. To make this concept as clear and simple as possible, I've chosen the first position of the A minor pentatomie
scale, which most guitarists are familiar with. The first figure below shows you the scale (#1), and the following scale (#2) was
created using half step neighbors going up into the.notes of the regular pentatonic shape or position. The #3 scale was creates
going down in half steps into the notes of the same pentatonic scale.

This riff has a very modern blues sound about it. There should be a difference between a blues guy and a jazz guy. The jazz
guy has more notes to pick from, and the audience wants that sort of experimentation. The blues guy is bound by tradition,
but don’t make the mistake of thinking he's limited. His job is to get as much out of fewer options, and that job is quite
harder. The blues audience doesn’t want to hear someone experimenting around. They want the blues tradition not to be
tampered with, so be careful when you use this stuff.

| always remind students that they should listen to the musicians that are known for the music they play. A lot of new students

ae INTERVAL JUMPING
LESSON #84: WITH RIFES

By “interval jumping with riffs,” | simply mean taking a line and repeating it at another position. You could move it a half step
(one fret), whole step (two frets), minor 3rd (three frets), major 3rd (four frets), etc. Many students of rock and blues often
wonder what to do with this stuff, and | always say, “Nothing.” This is primarily for the jazz musician. There’s countless ways to
take advantage of this knowledge. Take every riff you. play and move it up and down in different intervals. This is not for easy
listening jazz radio. This is for the contemporary, futuristic, modern musician.

Here we're moving up a minor 3rd (three frets).

EXAMPLE 1

—| 5—3 3--2-—_______} -4-
ee

Now we're moving down in minor 3rds (three frets).

How should we think of this? What is the justification for getting away with this? | always think of sliding up and down a string.
You hear guitarists doing that all the time. That's not a theory, but it’s a common effect. If you can slide up and down a string
and create a sound that’s usable, then why can't you riff up and down and create other types of usable sounds? Here’s how it
sounds moving a riff up and down in half steps. This riff is a pentatonic idea with a half step slide.

PLAYING DOWN
TWO WHOLE STEPS

LESSON #85:

We should be constantly looking at ways to resolve musical ideas especially when we're improvising. | took some private
lessons with Bob Conti, and he had me practice moving from the key of F to the key of A. F is two whole steps below A, and
that explains the title of this lesson. The way to explain the reason why this change works is a little more complicated, but
should be expressed for your understanding.

Two common resolutions were used to create these lines. The V chord in the key of A is E. We hear this common sound and
learn this theory from studying basic diatonic harmony, The E or E7 chord resolves smoothly into an A chord. A half step down
resolution is nice too. Going from an F chord to an E chord would be going down a half step, and this too is a common sound
in music. We can also use these concepts with regards to scales. Whatever changes we can make with chords we can make
with scales too. What makes these lines unusual sounding is that we skip any notes relating to the V chord (E). We start with a
run in F and jump to A. We've taken out the E, but we can still think of this lesson as F relating to E, which then resolves to A.
It's a bit of trickery, though; we skip any notes stating the E chord.

It's important to know in jazz that you don't have to play notes for every chord that comes up in a tune. Sometimes there are
several changes at fast tempos, and it seems impossible to keep up. You can spot chords and target your areas so you don't
have to worry about every chord, Just be sure to resolve your lines when you're heading to new keys or chords. Of course, this
takes lots of practice, but it makes what seemed impossible before easier and doable.

EXAMPLE 1

0:00 |

F major scale

A major scale

we're skipping E and jumping to A.

le) Ec: PLAYING OFF THE FIVE

An A major scale is spelled as follows: A (1)-B (2)-C# (3)-D (4)-E (5)-F# (6)-Ge (7). We sometimes see these represented with
Roman numerals (l=ii-iii-lV-V-vi-viie), especially when they represent the root notes of the chords built from those scale
tones. Playing “off the five” (or V) means you base your riffs on the 5th note of the key. In other words, we'd play in the key of E

when you're in A.

When I took my second lesson from Ted Greene, | hadn't seen him in over 20 years. He had given me so much to work on. |
felt as though | wasn't ready for any more information. | also owned both of his chord books, which could be studied over
a period of lifetimes. He raised his eyebrows and ask me if | needed to have the key that’s “up a 5th” (A to E) or down a 4th
(D to A) explained? Embarrassed to admit any lack of knowledge, | pretended | didn’t need to know, so he moved ahead to
harmonics, which was in answer to the question that | had put to him. For several months after my lesson, | pondered over
what he may have been getting at, and then one day in dawned on me; there's only one note difference between the key of A.
E, and D; D has two sharps, A has three, and E has four. Remember that we're talking about jazz, and jazz is meant to represent
free expression. So when you're jamming in A, you can improvise in D or E because these keys have only one note difference
between them, and that one note won't sound bad, especially if you're flying over passages. The following examples are meant
to really bring out a jazz quality using this knowledge of playing “off the five.”

This first example is E major played over an A7 harmony.

Since the guitar is a very visual instrument, | often say the whole tone scale looks like chicken wire. It has a repetitive pattern
that makes it easy to learn. The trouble with it is that it's very one-dimensional in sound. My first jazz teacher told me to stay
away from it because it’s too recognizable. | say that because in jazz we try and mask the scales we play. Hopefully people like
what we play without quite knowing what we're doing. There are ways to use it, but | prefer not to use it too much. The first
thing to understand is that there are really only two whole tone scales, and with that, you have the entire system covered, It’s
been created by using notes that are two frets (or one whole step) apart. If you move it up one fret, you have the next set of
notes for the other scale. Play these examples and try and see the relationships.

the same scale, just starting from a different note.

If we analyze one of our two whole tone scales, we have A-B-C+-D#-F-G, If you relate all the notes to the A note and use it as
the root, the relationships would be 1, 2, 3, 55, 25 (or $6), 67. Here are some of the pure chords you can create from this whole
tone scale: A7(no 5th), A9(no Sth), A7»5, A7#5, A945, A9#5, etc. Whatever chord you can create from these notes, you can play
the whole tone scale over the top. The strange thing about all this whole tone scale stuff is that this same breakdown of
harmony happens every two frets. In other words, there are six different keys covered by the same scale, You just pick the
root note and go up in whole steps and use the same breakdown of harmony. Any note in the scale can become the root
note. If you still don’t understand what I'm getting at, how’s this: if you move the scale up one fret, the other six keys are
covered with this theory. No wonder my first teacher wanted me to stay away from all this! It’s goofy!

way that makes it sound as though we were heading in that direction. The first time | heard about this is when | was studying
a Charlie Parker book. These kinds of sounds work really well in bebop or up-tempo swing. I've presented several practical
examples in the key of C major. Just remember that we're aiming for notes within the key of C, and this whole reality should

TWO ESSENTIAL
CHORD FORMS

LESSON #90:

The young or new student of jazz guitar usually sees the world of chords as a giant mountain to climb. When junior and high
school students come in for lessons, they at most times don’t know where to begin. This lesson is designed to get you started
learning the most practical chords for jazz band. Those students that are in a jazz band class for the first time need a system
that gets them covering the chord charts they're handed, and these charts usually contain chords they've never played or
heard before. We're not going to cover any theories here.

Notice that there are two chord shapes, or voicings, for each chord, One chord has its root or bass note on the sixth string and the
other on the fifth string. The reason for this is so that you can always have the next chord coming up in a progression close by.
You don't want to have to shift way up and down the fretboard all the time, especially when the song or tune has a fast tempo.

Example #1: Cmaj7 to Fmaj7. This is a big shift. You have to move seven fret positions. This chord change could still be used
and might sound more appropriate in certain situations. That decision is left entirely up to you unless the bandleader says
otherwise.

Cmaj7 Fmaj7
x x

- 8fr +

1 342 1 342

Example #2: Cmaj7 to Fmaj7. This is a close shift at the same fret position. This example shows you can make the chord
change without moving away from your current Position. It also gives you an alternative sound, which again might be more
appropriate.

Once you start getting these chords mastered and are able to play them more easily, the next thing to do is study the names
of the notes on the fifth and sixth strings, which is where the root notes of the chords are based. The fifth string starts with an
open A. The notes going up chromatically in half steps, or one fret at a time, are:

A - A?/B. - B- C - C2/D. - D - D#/B.- E-F - Fe/(G-- G-G#/AL-A

The # symbol = sharp (one fret up), and the + symbol = flat (one fret down). The tone is the same, but these notes have two
names because they‘re between two other tones. This occurrence is called enharmonic and is the same as the black keys of
the piano.

The sixth string starts with an open E. The notes going up chromatically in half steps are:
E - F - F#/G) — G - G#/A; - A - Az/B> - B- C - C#/Db - D - D#/E. - E

Get a guitar neck poster with the notes and put it up on the wall as soon as you can. Keep looking at these notes on the neck
on a regular basis until you become quick at recognizing and naming them. The next important point of all this is that ail
chord shapes are movable when you're not using open-string voicings. You know that all these chords in this lesson are C chords
right? If you move them up two frets, they become D chords of the same nature. The C7 chord moved up two frets becomes
a D7 chord. The C7 chord moved up four frets becomes an E7 chord, etc.

You could say you multiplied the chords you've learned in this lesson by twelve once you've moved them all up and down
the fretboard. You can do that and not even know what the names of the chords are, but that won't be good enough for jazz
band class. Get to know their names and positions, and you're on your way to becoming a proficient jazz guitarist that's 2
solid part of the rhythm section—the rhythm section being guitar, bass, piano, and drums.

has an F# instead of a G, as in the A7. There's only a half step or one fret difference.

100 JAZZ LESSONS | GUITAR LESSON GOLDMINE 185

el Es: y ta CHORD FAMILY TRAINING

The masters of jazz chords and jazz harmony have to be determined to familiarize themselves with the way chords sound, 2=
well as their construction. To deeply go into the study of this subject, we need a foundation as a Starting point. It's great t
be able to play chords you learn from books, but better to be able to construct arrangements of popular standards or original
compositions using your own knowledge and all the Possibilities it can give you. There may be many schools of thought as to
how to approach this subject, but this is how | was taught, and it has served me well.

For the thinking jazz musician, there are three types of chords, or three chord families: major, minor, and dominant. Each
group has a characteristic sound that we have to learn to recognize by ear. When drilling students, it’s usually easy for them to
identify the major and minor sounds. However, the dominant family somehow has to be easily recognized because it can be
strange sounding sometimes. But, it’s not that difficult once you hear how it’s distinguished: it just doesn't sound like a major or
minor chord!

Though we as guitarists don’t have the broad options and views a piano or keyboard can give, we can still comprehend
harmony from our own angle. To keep things simple and on track, we'll only be working in the key of C, which exists as the
white keys on the piano. A C major scale is spelled:

C=1 D=2/9 E=3 F=4/11 =5 A=6/13 B=7
Notice how D, F, and A can be represented by two different numbers? This is important in labeling chords with their proper

names. The C major family is founded on the C, E, and G notes—these being the 1, 3, and 5, or major triad.

The minor family is founded on C, B, and G notes—these being the 1, +3, and 5, or minor triad. Notice that, by only lowering
or flatting the E note a half step, we've created another chord family. In other words, the only difference between a major triad
and a minor triad is that the 3rd is a half step lower in a minor triad.

The dominant (-7) family is founded with the same C, E, and G, or 1-3-5 major triad, but it also has the b7th, which is a Bs note
in this case, added to the sound. This drives or throws the sound into another category.

To be practical, we'll work in one area of the fretboard and will only be dealing mainly with one octave. These are the three
foundational sounds or families of chords.

There are hundreds of other voicings for all three groups. To help simplify our thinking, all the altered, diminished, and
augmented chords are placed in the dominant category. As | said before, the reason for this is that they don’t sound like pure
major or minor chords. We want to be able to hear and identify chords, and this three-family logic really helps you do that.
There may be people who dispute this and say diminished and augmented chords have their own family, but here we are just
trying to train our ears to hear the difference—not cover the technical details necessary to label chord groups. Due to the
immense number of chords, this subject should be explored and covered in other books and lessons as well.

ele) Eka BUILDING ALTERED CHORDS

Students of jazz guitar always ask me, “What are all those weird chords with all the flat () and sharp (2) numbers?" To create an
altered chord, you can raise or lower the Sth or the 9th note by a half step. If we look at the formulas that go into the making
of chords, we can see how these weird chords come about.

C7 = 1-3-5-47, or C-E-G-B, taken from the scale of C major. The B> or+7th is characteristic of the foundation of this chord family.
Its presence opens the door for the free use of these altered notes. The notes of a C major scale are numbered as follows:

C1) D(2/9) E(3) F(4/11) G(5) A(6/13) B(7)
Please remember, however, that we're not using B here; we're using Bb.

For the sake of convenient fingerings and chords that are Possible to play, these voicings do not rely on perfect numerical
order and neither do they contain the complete 1-3-5 triad. The first chord, C7, is voiced B»-E-G-C, which would be the $7,
3, 5, and 1 (root) of the scale of C major. Arranging notes into different voicings gives musicians the freedom to create chord
sounds that have a personal touch and allow you to be unique.

ope (24; cms 78s C9noroot) C95(noroot) C9#5(noroot) C79noroot) C7F9(no root) C7;A(noroot) C7#(no root)

Notice how we've lowered the G note on the second string in order to create the C755 chord? And did you figure out that we
raised the G note up one fret to come up with the C745 chord? The G is the 5th note of the C major scale. That's why we call
it 5 or b5. The 45 can also be thought of as augmented. For the C9 chord, did you notice how we raised and lowered the D
note on the first string? The D is called the 9th instead of the 2nd, because the masters of theory wish to place it above the
1-3-5 triad, so they identify it going into the next octave higher up. Anyhow, can you see that we've raised and lowered it one
fret and also in combination with the altered Sth in some chords? Now let's try this same altered application with thirteenth
chords (1-3-5-47-9-13)! Again let me state that we're only using partial voicings in order to make the fingerings possible and
to continue with the logic of studying this subject in one small area on the fretboard.

The question always arises as to how far out we should go with these altered chords. For me, this group of A13 chords is a
good plateau and goal that should be mastered if you intend on performing several of the jazz standards found in the Real
Books (Hal Leonard). These sounds are identified with jazz and give your playing a level of sophistication. Once you can play
these, start learning them in different keys. Since we don’t incorporate any open strings, all these chords are movable. In
other words, if you move them up one fret, they become B+ or A? chords. To play most of these chords, you have to barre
patches of notes with different fingers. Everyone's hands are different, so experiment with the fingerings to find the most
comfortable grip. Good luck and get busy. As Ted Greene, the great jazz teacher, used to say, “Don't give up!”

Did you notice that the foundation of this group of A13 chords was b7-3-13. There's no 1 or 5 used from the A scale. This
makes these chords possible to play. We can use partial voicings to hint at the key we're in. It seems strange that we're playing
chords that don't have a root (1) or a 5th, but perhaps that’s the beauty of this music we call jazz. Jazz is fragments of sound
and editing traditional harmony in order to come up with a unique style of music.

The terms “circle of fourths” and/or “circle of fifths” are a common occurrence in music and, for the jazz musician, an important
study that enables you to implement altered chords into your music. To move a chord up a 4th is simple to explain. Using the
scale of C, let's count: C(1) D(2) E(3) F(4). From C to F is a 4th. So the chord change from C to F is up a 4th. It’s also five frets
or five half steps higher. If we continue up five more frets, we'll arrive at Bs. Then again from B up five frets to E is still yet
another 4th! By moving up in 4ths, we are leaving the key, but eventually we'll come back to the C where we started:

C-F-B)-B)-As-D)-G-B-E-A-D-G-C

We cycled all the way back to C using the circle of fourths. The amazing thing about this is that if we use each note as 2
root note for a chord, it allows us to theoretically apply any chord we want. | say theoretically because we still have to use
intuition or taste in choosing the chords, but this system of cycling through 4ths gives you the format. In this example, |
cycled through six changes of 4ths and then resolved from D»13 to C6/9, which is the key we started with. | didn't have to go
through the entire cycle to get back to C major.

Se) Es) CHORD-CHANGE KEY

Ths 50m is about the possibilities and options you have when chords get moved a certain interval. (In regards to the word
“Ney” | don't mean the key of music; | mean the key to using chords—like the key to unlock the wisdom of correct harmonic
iis & terms of using chords in jazz.) There are only five types of chord changes measured by intervals in this lesson:

1. Moving chords down a half step (one fret) 4. Moving chords up a 4th (five frets)

2 Moving chords down a minor 3rd (three frets) 5. Moving chords up a}5th (six frets)

3. Moving chords up a minor 3rd (three frets)
© Chords are moved the distance of any of these intervals or changes, the following chord can be any chord you want it to be.
Sor exemple, you can move any A chord up a 4th to any D chord (e.g,, A13 to D745 or Amaj7 to Dm7}5, etc.). These devices give

= composer options in selecting chords. The five different intervals I've selected for this purpose are for me the easiest to
2m0y and are very common in the tunes and songs we learn.

Te a= in the habit of seeing your choices, I've constructed some graphic charts. The bass or root note of the chord you're
deserting from is the circle or blank dot. The black dots are the bass or root notes you have the choice of moving to. Please
understand that these are the five changes based on the root note of each chord.

FUNDAMENTAL
CHORD KNOWLEDGE

LESSON #97:

The jazz guitar style demands a thorough study. If you really love jazz music and the beautiful sounds of the chords and the
melodies, there's no.getting around it; you have to work hard at it. Knowing chord inversions up and down the neck and how
to support a melody with them is a key principle in jazz guitar, especially when you want to play solo pieces and have them
sound full and complete. This lesson is designed to give you an idea of the practical knowledge needed in order to achieve
some adequate results.

There are limitless ways to approach every song, and there are so many great contributors: Joe Pass, Ted Greene, Barney Kessel,
Johnny Smith, to name a few. The main thing to remember in chord melody is to always have the melody of the tune or song
on top of the chords as the highest sounding pitch. This is similar to a piano player playing the melody with the right hand
and the chords with the left.

This example is a tune | wrote for this lesson. Let's call it “Windy Friday" since it's gusting outside of my house right now. | wrote
a melody and selected some chords to go with it. If we look at jazz standards in sheet music collection books called “Real
Books” or “Fake Books,” this is how the tunes get presented to you. They give you the melody and chords, and that's it. It’s up
to us to decide how we're going to play the music. You could work on the chords separately and then work on the melody or
lines. If you're in the jazz band at school, they may want you to only play the chords. If you play the saxophone, they usually
have you play the “head” (melody).

Get familiar with this melody. I've provided you with tablature if your reading skills haven't been developed yet. Most of the
jazz manuals don't have tablature (tab), so eventually you need to bite the bullet and learn how to read well enough to be
able to figure out a line or a melody. If you don’t know these chords, you should look them up in chord reference books to
Prove to yourself you're capable of studying music the same way youd study science or math. No one is going to do it for you.

Normally you don’t see guitar tablature given with the notation of a song. There are publications that have tab, but | highly
recommend you not rely on it 24/7. Start learning at least some basic reading skills. There are many easy reading method books
for guitar, and you'll be much more prepared to learn songs for chord melody just by gaining some fundamental knowledge.

All the chords here are written in 8th notes for the sake of separation, enabling you to see the chord and melody more clearly.
However, you don't have to chop the sound of the chord off; you may allow it to sustain longer while you add or move to the
next melody note. My favorite sound on the guitar is hearing a chord being sustained while a melody is being played across
the top. The first time | heard that | thought it was impossible and asked myself, “How can a sustaining chord and a melody
get played on the guitar at the same time? Wouldn't you need four hands and two guitars to do that?”

Perhaps it was George Van Eps who originally started developing the guitar's chordal possibilities in this modern age? Wes
Montgomery claimed he didn’t have command of chords, but if you listen to his music, the chords are beautiful and seem
to be perfectly selected. Since Wes only used his thumb, he probably thought he didn’t do enough with his delivery of
chords. Since a chord can be strummed or plucked with your fingers or a pick, different techniques have been applied to
this medium. With the advent of YouTube, we can watch videos of people and study from their example. | feel I've been
influenced by everybody, and | don't really have a favorite. Ted Greene, Joe Pass, Bob Conti, Wes Montgomery, Ron Eschete,
Jack Wilkins, Chet Atkins, Chuck Wayne, George Benson, and Grant Green are some of the many outstanding musicians that
have inspired and affected me on a deep level. When | say “deep level,” | mean they changed the course of my life and sent
me ona relentless quest in an attempt to play good music, leaving all other considerations as secondary, except for my wife
and kids.

a= \e) E>}: DOING YOUR OWN VERSION

The a= Many personal choices in writing chord melody. In an attempt to teach some of the fundamentals, I'm going to give
= @=p-2y-step explanation of what's going on in my head as | select chords to accompany a melody. This, of course, is all going
% De Gore with the desired result being a jazz-flavored arrangement of the tune. There's classical, pop, Americana, Latin, just
® meme = few styles, but in order for it to sound like jazz, there are some tricks we should apply. Most of the melody will be
@ Me wey Of C, but an important thing to notice is that a lot of the chords will not be in that key; and the question arises as to
Smee why we can do that? When we harmonize the C major scale in seventh chords, we get Cmaj7, Dm7, Em7, Fmaj7, G7, Am7,
anc Se7-5. We should also know that another way to identify these chords is with Roman numerals, which would be Imaj7,
HT G7, Vemaj7, V7, vim7, and viim735.

Je Seo! with, we need a melody. | just created this line in C major while | was sitting here to give us something to work with.

EXAMPLE 1

Moderately slow

What we need now is a starting chord with a B note on top. Another way to say that, in case you don’t understand, would be
that the B note is the highest sounding note of the chord; that chord could be any chord you want to pick. You just have to
dive in and go for some kind of chord with a B note as the highest note. | picked a rootless D13 chord.

EXAMPLE 2

4

D13 G7sus4 C9 Fmaj? Em7  A9  D7b9Gmaj7_F#7b9  Fmaj7 Bb7b5 A7#S — G#7b5 Gi3sus4 C7sus4 F7b5 C6

200 100 JAZZ LESSONS | GUITAR LESSON GOLDMINE

! will now to the best of my ability explain how this chord arrangement came about. | will mention again that all the
highest sounding notes are the melody notes of our song. Another thing to take into account is that every chord has been
embellished. In other words, instead of playing a C major chord, we've made it a C6. Instead of playing an E minor chord,
we've played an Em7, etc. This type of thinking is what | call “Jazz 101.” You can exchange, replace, or substitute any chord with
another chord from the same family.

} D13:1 picked D13 because it had a B note on top, and thirteenth chords always sound jazzy to my ears. I've wanted
to write a book about the thirteenth because guitarists tend to avoid that study. By the time we've learned dozens of
other chords, the thirteenth chord often goes by the wayside.

} G7sus4: This chord is a 4th higher than the D13 chord. The beauty of the 4th principle in making a change with
chords is that it allows you to pick any chord. Why? The answer is resolution. It’s a nice resolution to move chords by a
4th (D to G, G to C, C to F, F to Bs, etc.). Also, our melody notes are on the top of this chord.

} C9: C9 isa 4th higher than G7sus4, so the resolution is smooth sounding, and the melody of our tune is on the top.
} Fmaj7: This chord is up another 4th (C to F) with the melody on the top.

} Em7: The Em7 is down a half step (one fret), which is another smooth sounding resolution in music. Again, as with
4ths, half steps allow you to pick any chord you want. When | say any chord, | mean 99% of the time, because you still
have to use taste. Ever hear anyone say, “Man that guy’s a tasty guitarist!” It means the sounds he or she comes up
with are appealing.

} A9: This is up a 4th from Em7 with the melody on top.

} D7:9: This is up a 4th from A9 with the melody on top.

» Gmaj7: It's up a 4th from D7+9 with the melody on top. Do you see a pattern here?
) F#7+9: This chord is down a half step from Gmaj7.

} Fmaj7: This one is down a half step from F479.

Don't forget that the melody note is on top and that all these chords have been embellished, Use chords that have been
enhanced. Instead of C7, use C9 or C13!

) BL715: This is up a 4th from Fmaj7.

) A7#5: This chord is down a half step Bb7)5,

} G#745: This is down another half step. Do you notice all the altered chords (15, #5, b9, 29)?

} G13sus4: We're down another half step

» C7sus4: This is up a 4th from G13sus4.

) F715: This is up a 4th from C7sus4.

} C6: This chord is up a Sth from F745. (Note that F7+5 could also be a rootless G95, which is a 4th below C6.)

For me, there are two main areas for chord-change consideration. One of those areas involves common root movements,
such as up a 4th, down a step, etc. The other is what | call diatonic changes. Diatonic changes are the ones found within the
key. In other words, the chords of C major are Cmaj7, Dm7, Em7, Fmaj7, G7, Am7, and Bm7+5. Any changes that take place
using only those chords are diatonic changes. They appear a great deal in songwriting, but jazz musicians will tamper with
those straight-sounding or bland vanilla chords to come up with a jazzier sound.

100 JAZZ LESSONS | GUITAR LESSON GOLDMINE 201

Ble) Eaieleh EXTENDED MELODIES

he & think that I'm always having a conversation with myself when it comes to playing riffs or melodies—especially when
t= pieyine an extended or long phrase. When | was first getting into this, | was casually doodling around on my guitar in the
sShmemoom at a friend's business location. | was just sitting there practicing when a customer came in and, while shopping, he
ist=nec to me playing. After he was done, out of the blue he said, “Your music has to tell a story,’ and that was it. We can read
gout these concepts and ideas about how to get good, but when you're in the middle of trying to produce good music and a
eae Clearly points to a new direction, you've got to take note. The word “phrasing” comes up a lot. Musical sense really hasn't
got any words, but the difference between good phrasing and poor seems to be good storytelling with your instrument.

Some you like the lines I've come up with. know not everyone may think these are good jazz riffs. When it comes to music,
people have different ideas and views as to what's good and bad. Bill Cosby said he didn’t know the formula for success, but
he kmew the formula for failure: trying to please everybody. Jazz can go over everyone's head, and a great part of the world’s
populstion doesn't get it. If you like jazz and want to play this music, it’s a lifetime of study and hard work. What's in it for you
& Qreat music and a sense of infinite creativity.

Where you see the stated chord symbols is where the feeling of the phrases change. You could say I'm changing keys, but I'm
not. When | play in a trio, the bass notes could be looked at as single root notes without any specific chord center, thus giving
you the freedom to go off in different harmonic directions.

When you look at the key signature, it says C major. Do you notice the amount of accidentals (flats and sharps) there are? |
like the sound of soloing through different scales while the bass is pedaling a note or staying in a key. When a guy like Charlie
Parker can come up with all sorts of melodic jazz lines that people haven't heard before the question arises, “Where did they
come from? Where did he get his ideas from?” The answer is everywhere. He listened to every different style of music. He
experimented with his horn until he started to create the sounds he was going for in his head. It’s not all going to come from
an external source. Jazz was meant to be a spontaneous expression of the musician's ideas. You have to try and try again to
play what you're hearing in your head. Frank Zappa said, “If | get close to the idea in my head, that's good enough because
the audience can’t hear what I'm thinking.”

WOU 52- FUNK

This series will help you play your favorite songs
quickly and easily. Just follow the tab and listen to the B
CD to the hear how the guitar should sound, and then play along
using the separate backing tracks. Mac or PC users can also slow
down the tempo without changing pitch by using the CD in their
computer. The melody and lyrics are included in the book so that
you can sing or simply follow along.

GUITAR LESSON

GOLOMINE

Expand your guitar knowledge with the GUITAR LESSON GOLDMINE series! Featuring
100 individual modules covering a giant array of topics, each lesson in this JAZZ volume includes
detailed instruction with playing examples presented in standard notation and tablature. You'll also get
extremely useful tips, scale diagrams, chord grids, and more to reinforce your learning experience, plus

two audio CDs featuring performance demos of all the examples in the book!

A huge variety of jazz guitar styles and techniques are covered, including:

* MODES * MAJOR TO MINOR ° MELODIC MINOR

° ARPEGGIOS ¢ PLAYING THE CHANGES ¢ SUPER LOCRIAN

¢ BASIC COMPING * RESOLUTIONS ° NEIGHBOR TONES

¢ BLUES COMPING ¢ CHORDAL SHELLS ¢ STRING SKIPPING

* TURNAROUND IMPROVISATION ¢ FREDDIE GREEN COMPING ° GEOMETRIC DESIGN

ell-V-l ¢ BOSSA NOVA RHYTHM ° INTERVAL JUMPING

* CHORD TONES ° OCTAVES ° PLAYING OFF THE V CHORD
¢ CHORD FUNDAMENTALS ° SUS CHORDS * WHOLE TONE SCALE

* TRITONE SUBSTITUTION ¢ JAZZ HEMIOLA * ENCLOSURES

¢ TRITONE TURNAROUNDS ° SLASH CHORDS * CHORD SYSTEM

¢ MINOR BLUES ° QUARTALS * CHORD FAMILIES

* CALL AND RESPONSE ° BALLADS * BUILDING ALTERED CHORDS
¢ JAZZ-BLUES LICKS ¢ POLYPHONIC HARMONY ° FREE FORM PLAYING

¢ MINI SWEEPS ¢ DISSONANCE ° 3/4 SOLOING

¢ SCALE SEQUENCES ° CHROMATIC RIFFS ° ALTERNATE PICKING

° CHORD SHAPE LINES ¢ DIMINISHED FLUENCY °¢ AND MUCH MORE!

¢ PENTATONICS ¢ HARMONIC MINOR
